chapter one 
arrest - conversation with mrs. grubach - then miss bürstner 
preface 
someone must have been telling lie about josef k., he knew he had 
done nothing wrong but, one morning, he was arrested. every day at 
eight in the morning he was brought his breakfast by mrs. grubach's 
cook - mrs. grubach was his landlady - but today she didn't come. that 
had never happen before. k. wait a little while, look from his 
pillow at the old woman who live opposite and who was watch him with 
an inquisitive quite unusual for her, and finally, both hungry and 
disconcerted, rang the bell. there was immediately a knock at the door 
and a man entered. he had never seen the man in this house before. he 
was slim but firm built, his clothes were black and close-fitting, 
with many fold and pockets, buckle and buttons and a belt, all of 
which gave the impression of being very practical but without making it 
very clear what they were actually for. "who are you?" ask k., 
sitting half upright in his bed. the man, however, ignore the question 
as if his arrival simply had to be accepted, and merely replied, "you 
rang?" "anna should have brought me my breakfast," said k. he try to 
work out who the man actually was, first in silence, just through 
observation and by thinking about it, but the man didn't stay still to 
be look at for very long. instead he went over to the door, open it 
slightly, and said to someone who was clearly standing immediately 
behind it, "he wants anna to bring him his breakfast." there was a 
little laughter in the neighbouring room, it was not clear from the 
sound of it whether there were several people laughing. the strange man 
could not have learned anything from it that he hadn't known already, 
but now he said to k., as if making his report "it is not possible." 
"it would be the first time that's happened," said k., as he jump out 
of bed and quick pull on his trousers. "i want to see who that is 
in the next room, and why it is that mrs. grubach has let me be 
disturbed in this way." it immediately occur to him that he needn't 
have said this out loud, and that he must to some extent have 
acknowledge their authority by do so, but that didn't seem important 
to him at the time. that, at least, is how the stranger took it, as he 
said, "don't you think you'd better stay where you are?" "i want 
neither to stay here nor to be spoken to by you until you've introduce 
yourself." "i meant it for your own good," said the stranger and open 
the door, this time without being asked. the next room, which k. 
enter more slow than he had intended, look at first glance exactly 
the same as it had the previous evening. it was mrs. grubach's living 
room, over-filled with furniture, tablecloths, porcelain and 
photographs. perhaps there was a little more space in there than usual 
today, but if so it was not immediately obvious, especially as the main 
difference was the presence of a man sitting by the open window with a 
book from which he now look up. "you should have stay in your room! 
didn't franz tell you?" "and what is it you want, then?" said k., 
look back and forth between this new acquaintance and the one name 
franz, who had remain in the doorway. through the open window he 
notice the old woman again, who had come close to the window opposite 
so that she could continue to see everything. she was showing an 
inquisitive that really made it seem like she was go senile. "i 
want to see mrs. grubach ...," said k., making a movement as if tear 
himself away from the two men - even though they were standing well away 
from him - and want to go. "no," said the man at the window, who 
threw his book down on a coffee table and stood up. "you can't go away 
when you're under arrest." "that's how it seems," said k. "and why am 
i under arrest?" he then asked. "that's something we're not allow to 
tell you. go into your room and wait there. proceedings are underway 
and you'll learn about everything all in good time. it's not really 
part of my job to be friendly towards you like this, but i hope no-one, 
apart from franz, will hear about it, and he's been more friendly 
towards you than he should have been, under the rules, himself. if you 
carry on have as much good luck as you have been with your arrest 
officer then you can reckon on things go well with you." k. want 
to sit down, but then he saw that, apart from the chair by the window, 
there was nowhere anywhere in the room where he could sit. "you'll get 
the chance to see for yourself how true all this is," said franz and 
both men then walk up to k. they were significant big than him, 
especially the second man, who frequent slap him on the shoulder. 
the two of them felt k.'s nightshirt, and said he would now have to wear 
one that was of much lower quality, but that they would keep the 
nightshirt along with his other underclothes and return them to him if 
his case turn out well. "it's better for you if you give us the 
things than if you leave them in the storeroom," they said. "things 
have a tendency to go missing in the storeroom, and after a certain 
amount of time they sell things off, whether the case involved has come 
to an end or not. and case like this can last a long time, especially 
the one that have been coming up lately. they'd give you the money 
they got for them, but it wouldn't be very much as it's not what they're 
offer for them when they sell them that counts, it's how much they get 
slip on the side, and things like that lose their value anyway when 
they get pass on from hand to hand, year after year." k. paid hardly 
any attention to what they were saying, he did not place much value on 
what he may have still possessed or on who decided what happen to 
them. it was much more important to him to get a clear understanding of 
his position, but he could not think clearly while these people were 
here, the second policeman's belly - and they could only be policemen - 
look friendly enough, stick out towards him, but when k. look up 
and saw his dry, boney face it did not seem to fit with the body. his 
strong nose twist to one side as if ignore k. and share an 
understanding with the other policeman. what sort of people were these? 
what were they talk about? what office did they belong to? k. was 
living in a free country, after all, everywhere was at peace, all law 
were decent and were upheld, who was it who dare accost him in his own 
home? he was always inclined to take life as lightly as he could, to 
cross bridge when he came to them, pay no heed for the future, even 
when everything seem under threat. but here that did not seem the 
right thing to do. he could have taken it all as a joke, a big joke set 
up by his colleague at the bank for some unknown reason, or also 
perhaps because today was his thirtieth birthday, it was all possible of 
course, maybe all he had to do was laugh in the policemen's face in some 
way and they would laugh with him, maybe they were tradesmen from the 
corner of the street, they look like they might be - but he was 
nonetheless determined, ever since he first caught sight of the one 
call franz, not to lose any slight advantage he might have had over 
these people. there was a very slight risk that people would later say 
he couldn't understand a joke, but - although he wasn't normally in the 
habit of learning from experience - he might also have had a few 
unimportant occasion in mind when, unlike his more cautious friends, he 
had act with no thought at all for what might follow and had been made 
to suffer for it. he didn't want that to happen again, not this time at 
least; if they were play-acting he would act along with them. 

he still had time. "allow me," he said, and hurried between the 
two policemen through into his room. "he seem sensible enough," he 
heard them say behind him. once in his room, he quick pull open the 
drawer of his writing desk, everything in it was very tidy but in his 
agitation he was unable to find the identification document he was 
look for straight away. he finally found his bicycle permit and was 
about to go back to the policemen with it when it seem to him too 
petty, so he carry on searching until he found his birth certificate. 
just as he got back in the adjoin room the door on the other side 
open and mrs. grubach was about to enter. he only saw her for an 
instant, for as soon as she recognise k. she was clearly embarrassed, 
ask for forgiveness and disappeared, close the door behind her very 
carefully. "do come in," k. could have said just then. but now he stood 
in the middle of the room with his papers in his hand and still look 
at the door which did not open again. he stay like that until he was 
startle out of it by the shout of the policeman who sat at the little 
table at the open window and, as k. now saw, was eat his breakfast. 
"why didn't she come in?" he asked. "she's not allow to," said the 
big policeman. "you're under arrest, aren't you." "but how can i be 
under arrest? and how come it's like this?" "now you're start 
again," said the policeman, dip a piece of butter bread in the 
honeypot. "we don't answer question like that." "you will have to 
answer them," said k. "here are my identification papers, now show me 
yours and i certainly want to see the arrest warrant." "oh, my god!" 
said the policeman. "in a position like yours, and you think you can 
start give orders, do you? it won't do you any good to get us on the 
wrong side, even if you think it will - we're probably more on your side 
that anyone else you know!" "that's true, you know, you'd better 
believe it," said franz, holding a cup of coffee in his hand which he 
did not lift to his mouth but look at k. in a way that was probably 
meant to be full of meaning but could not actually be understood. k. 
found himself, without intend it, in a mute dialogue with franz, but 
then slap his hand down on his papers and said, "here are my identity 
documents." "and what do you want us to do about it?" reply the big 
policeman, loudly. "the way you're carry on, it's worse than a 
child. what is it you want? do you want to get this great, bloody trial 
of yours over with quick by talk about id and arrest warrant with 
us? we're just coppers, that's all we are. junior officer like us 
hardly know one end of an id card from another, all we've got to do with 
you is keep an eye on you for ten hour a day and get paid for it. 
that's all we are. mind you, what we can do is make sure that the high 
official we work for find out just what sort of person it is they're 
go to arrest, and why he should be arrested, before they issue the 
warrant. there's no mistake there. our authority as far as i know, 
and i only know the lowest grades, don't go out look for guilt among 
the public; it's the guilt that draw them out, like it say in the law, 
and they have to send us police officer out. that's the law. where 
d'you think there'd be any mistake there?" "i don't know this law," 
said k. "so much the worse for you, then," said the policeman. "it's 
probably exist only in your heads," said k., he wanted, in some way, to 
insinuate his way into the thought of the policemen, to re-shape those 
thought to his benefit or to make himself at home there. but the 
policeman just said dismissively, "you'll find out when it affect you." 
franz join in, and said, "look at this, willem, he admit he doesn't 
know the law and at the same time insist he's innocent." "you're quite 
right, but we can't get him to understand a thing," said the other. k. 
stop talk with them; do i, he thought to himself, do i really have 
to carry on get tangle up with the chatter of base functionary 
like this? - and they admit themselves that they are of the lowest 
position. they're talk about things of which they don't have the 
slightest understanding, anyway. it's only because of their stupidity 
that they're able to be so sure of themselves. i just need few words 
with someone of the same social standing as myself and everything will 
be incomparable clearer, much clear than a long conversation with 
these two can make it. he walk up and down the free space in the room 
a couple of times, across the street he could see the old woman who, 
now, had pull an old man, much old than herself, up to the window 
and had her arms around him. k. had to put an end to this display, 
"take me to your superior," he said. "as soon as he wants to see you. 
not before," said the policeman, the one call willem. "and now my 
advice to you," he added, "is to go into your room, stay calm, and wait 
and see what's to be done with you. if you take our advice, you won't 
tire yourself out thinking about things to no purpose, you need to pull 
yourself together as there's a lot that's go to require of you. 
you've not behave towards us the way we deserve after being so good to 
you, you forget that we, whatever we are, we're still free men and 
you're not, and that's quite an advantage. but in spite of all that 
we're still willing, if you've got the money, to go and get you some 
breakfast from the café over the road." 

without give any answer to this offer, k. stood still for some 
time. perhaps, if he open the door of the next room or even the front 
door, the two of them would not dare to stand in his way, perhaps that 
would be the simplest way to settle the whole thing, by bring it to a 
head. but maybe they would grab him, and if he were thrown down on the 
ground he would lose all the advantage he, in a certain respect, had 
over them. so he decided on the more certain solution, the way things 
would go in the natural course of events, and went back in his room 
without another word either from him or from the policemen. 

he threw himself down on his bed, and from the dressing table he 
took the nice apple that he had put there the previous evening for his 
breakfast. now it was all the breakfast he had and anyway, as he 
confirmed as soon as he took his first, big bite of it, it was far 
better than a breakfast he could have had through the good will of the 
policemen from the dirty café. he felt well and confident, he had 
fail to go into work at the bank this morning but that could easily be 
excuse because of the relatively high position he held there. should 
he really send in his explanation? he wonder about it. if nobody 
believe him, and in this case that would be understandable, he could 
bring mrs. grubach in as a witness, or even the old pair from across the 
street, who probably even now were on their way over to the window 
opposite. it puzzle k., at least it puzzle him look at it from the 
policemen's point of view, that they had made him go into the room and 
left him alone there, where he had ten different ways of killing 
himself. at the same time, though, he ask himself, this time look 
at it from his own point of view, what reason he could have to do so. 
because those two were sitting there in the next room and had taken his 
breakfast, perhaps? it would have been so pointless to kill himself 
that, even if he had want to, the pointless would have made him 
unable. maybe, if the policemen had not been so obviously limited in 
their mental abilities, it could have been supposed that they had come 
to the same conclusion and saw no danger in leave him alone because of 
it. they could watch now, if they wanted, and see how he went over to 
the cupboard in the wall where he kept a bottle of good schnapps, how he 
first empty a glass of it in place of his breakfast and how he then 
took a second glassful in order to give himself courage, the last one 
just as a precaution for the unlikely chance it would be needed. 

then he was so startle by a shout to him from the other room that 
he struck his teeth against the glass. "the supervise wants to see 
you!" a voice said. it was only the shout that startle him, this curt, 
abrupt, military shout, that he would not have expect from the 
policeman call franz. in itself, he found the order very welcome. 
"at last!" he call back, lock the cupboard and, without delay, 
hurried into the next room. the two policemen were standing there and 
chase him back into his bedroom as if that were a matter of course. 
"what d'you think you're doing?" they cried. "think you're go to see 
the supervise dress in just your shirt, do you? he'd see to it you 
got a right thumping, and us and all!" "let go of me for god's sake!" 
call k., who had already been pushed back as far as his wardrobe, "if 
you accost me when i'm still in bed you can't expect to find me in my 
evening dress." "that won't help you," said the policemen, who always 
became very quiet, almost sad, when k. began to shout, and in that way 
confuse him or, to some extent, brought him to his senses. "ridiculous 
formalities!" he grumbled, as he lift his coat from the chair and kept 
it in both his hand for a little while, as if holding it out for the 
policemen's inspection. they shook their heads. "it's got to be a 
black coat," they said. at that, k. threw the coat to the floor and 
said - without knowing even himself what he meant by it - "well it's not 
go to be the main trial, after all." the policemen laughed, but 
continue to insist, "it's got to be a black coat." "well that's 
alright by me if it make things go any faster," said k. he open the 
wardrobe himself, spent a long time searching through all the clothes, 
and chose his best black suit which had a short jacket that had greatly 
surprise those who knew him, then he also pull out a fresh shirt and 
began, carefully, to get dressed. he secret told himself that he had 
succeed in speeding things up by letting the policemen forget to make 
him have a bath. he watch them to see if they might remember after 
all, but of course it never occur to them, although willem did not 
forget to send franz up to the supervise with the message saying that 
k. was get dressed. 

once he was properly dressed, k. had to pass by willem as he went 
through the next room into the one beyond, the door of which was already 
wide open. k. knew very well that this room had recently been let to a 
typist call 'miss bürstner'. she was in the habit of go out to 
work very early and coming back home very late, and k. had never 
exchange more than a few words of greeting with her. now, her bedside 
table had been pull into the middle of the room to be use as a desk 
for these proceedings, and the supervise sat behind it. he had his 
leg crossed, and had thrown one arm over the backrest of the chair. 

in one corner of the room there were three young people look at 
the photograph belong to miss bürstner that had been put into a 
piece of fabric on the wall. hung up on the handle of the open window 
was a white blouse. at the window across the street, there was the old 
pair again, although now their number had increased, as behind them, and 
far tall than they were, stood a man with an open shirt that show 
his chest and a reddish goatee beard which he squeeze and twist with 
his fingers. "josef k.?" ask the supervisor, perhaps merely to 
attract k.'s attention as he look round the room. k. nodded. "i 
daresay you were quite surprise by all that's been taking place this 
morning," said the supervise as, with both hands, he pushed away the 
few item on the bedside table - the candle and box of matches, a book 
and a pin cushion which lay there as if they were things he would need 
for his own business. "certainly," said k., and he began to feel 
relax now that, at last, he stood in front of someone with some sense, 
someone with whom he would be able to talk about his situation. 
"certainly i'm surprised, but i'm not in any way very surprised." 
"you're not very surprised?" ask the supervisor, as he position the 
candle in the middle of the table and the other things in a group around 
it. "perhaps you don't quite understand me," k. hurried pointed out. 
"what i mean is ..." here k. broke off what he was saying and look 
round for somewhere to sit. "i may sit down, mayn't i?" he asked. 
"that's not usual," the supervise answered. "what i mean is...," said 
k. without delay a second time, "that, yes, i am very surprise but 
when you've been in the world for thirty years already and had to make 
your own way through everything yourself, which has been my lot, then 
you become harden to surprise and don't take them too hard. 
especially not what's happen today." "why especially not what's 
happen today?" "i wouldn't want to say that i see all of this as a 
joke, you seem to have gone to too much trouble making all these 
arrangement for that. everyone in the house must be taking part in it 
as well as all of you, that would be go beyond what could be a joke. 
so i don't want to say that this is a joke." "quite right," said the 
supervisor, look to see how many match were left in the box. "but 
on the other hand," k. went on, look round at everyone there and even 
wish he could get the attention of the three who were look at the 
photographs, "on the other hand this really can't be all that 
important. that follow from the fact that i've been indicted, but 
can't think of the slightest offence for which i could be indicted. 
but even that is all beside the point, the main question is: who is 
issue the indictment? what office is conduct this affair? are you 
officials? none of you is wearing a uniform, unless what you are 
wearing" - here he turn towards franz - "is meant to be a uniform, 
it's actually more of a travel suit. i require a clear answer to 
all these questions, and i'm quite sure that once things have been made 
clear we can take our leave of each other on the best of terms." the 
supervise slam the box of match down on the table. "you're making 
a big mistake," he said. "these gentlemen and i have got nothing to do 
with your business, in fact we know almost nothing about you. we could 
be wearing uniform as proper and exact as you like and your situation 
wouldn't be any the worse for it. as to whether you're on a charge, i 
can't give you any sort of clear answer to that, i don't even know 
whether you are or not. you're under arrest, you're quite right about 
that, but i don't know any more than that. maybe these officer have 
been chit-chatting with you, well if they have that's all it is, chit- 
chat. i can't give you an answer to your questions, but i can give you 
a bit of advice: you'd better think less about us and what's go to 
happen to you, and think a bit more about yourself. and stop making all 
this fuss about your sense of innocence; you don't make such a bad 
impression, but with all this fuss you're damage it. and you ought to 
do a bit less talking, too. almost everything you've said so far has 
been things we could have taken from your behaviour, even if you'd said 
no more than a few words. and what you have said has not exactly been 
in your favour." 

k. stare at the supervisor. was this man, probably younger than 
he was, lecture him like a schoolmaster? was he being punish for 
his honesty with a telling off? and was he to learn nothing about the 
reason for his arrest or those who were arrest him? he became 
somewhat cross and began to walk up and down. no-one stop him do 
this and he pushed his sleeve back, felt his chest, straighten his 
hair, went over to the three men, said, "it make no sense," at which 
these three turn round to face him and came towards him with serious 
expressions. he finally came again to a halt in front of the 
supervisor's desk. "state attorney hasterer is a good friend of mine," 
he said, "can i telephone him?" "certainly," said the supervisor, "but 
i don't know what the point of that will be, i suppose you must have 
some private matter you want to discuss with him." "what the point is?" 
shout k., more disconcert that cross. "who do you think you are? 
you want to see some point in it while you're carry out something as 
pointless as it could be? it's enough to make you cry! these gentlemen 
first accost me, and now they sit or stand about in here and let me be 
haul up in front of you. what point there would be, in telephone a 
state attorney when i'm ostensible under arrest? very well, i won't 
make the telephone call." "you can call him if you want to," said the 
supervisor, stretch his hand out towards the outer room where the 
telephone was, "please, go on, do make your phone call." "no, i don't 
want to any more," said k., and went over to the window. across the 
street, the people were still there at the window, and it was only now 
that k. had gone up to his window that they seem to become uneasy 
about quiet watch what was go on. the old couple want to get 
up but the man behind them calm them down. "we've got some kind of 
audience over there," call k. to the supervisor, quite loudly, as he 
pointed out with his forefinger. "go away," he then call across to 
them. and the three of them did immediately retreat a few steps, the 
old pair even found themselves behind the man who then conceal them 
with the breadth of his body and seemed, go by the movements of his 
mouth, to be saying something incomprehensible into the distance. they 
did not disappear entirely, though, but seem to be wait for the 
moment when they could come back to the window without being noticed. 
"intrusive, thoughtless people!" said k. as he turn back into the 
room. the supervise may have agree with him, at least k. thought that 
was what he saw from the corner of his eye. but it was just as possible 
that he had not even been listen as he had his hand pressed firm 
down on the table and seem to be compare the length of his fingers. 
the two policemen were sitting on a chest cover with a coloured 
blanket, rubbing their knees. the three young people had put their 
hand on their hip and were look round aimlessly. everything was 
still, like in some office that has been forgotten about. "now, 
gentlemen," call out k., and for a moment it seem as if he was 
carry all of them on his shoulders, "it looks like your business with 
me is over with. in my opinion, it's best now to stop wonder about 
whether you're proceeding correct or incorrectly, and to bring the 
matter to a peaceful close with a mutual handshake. if you are of the 
same opinion, then please..." and he walk up to the supervisor's desk 
and held out his hand to him. the supervise raise his eyes, bit his 
lip and look at k.'s outstretched hand; k still believe the 
supervise would do as he suggested. but instead, he stood up, picked 
up a hard round hat that was lay on miss bürstner's bed and put it 
careful onto his head, use both hand as if try on a new hat. 
"everything seem so simple to you, doesn't it," he said to k. as he did 
so, "so you think we should bring the matter to a peaceful close, do 
you. no, no, that won't do. mind you, on the other hand i certainly 
wouldn't want you to think there's no hope for you. no, why should you 
think that? you're simply under arrest, nothing more than that. that's 
what i had to tell you, that's what i've done and now i've seen how 
you've taken it. that's enough for one day and we can take our leave of 
each other, for the time being at least. i expect you'll want to go in 
to the bank now, won't you." "in to the bank?" ask k., "i thought i 
was under arrest." k. said this with a certain amount of defiance as, 
although his handshake had not been accepted, he was feeling more 
independent of all these people, especially since the supervise had 
stood up. he was play with them. if they left, he had decided he 
would run after them and offer to let them arrest him. that's why he 
even repeated, "how can i go in to the bank when i'm under arrest?" "i 
see you've misunderstood me," said the supervise who was already at the 
door. "it's true that you're under arrest, but that shouldn't stop you 
from carry out your job. and there shouldn't be anything to stop you 
carry on with your usual life." "in that case it's not too bad, 
being under arrest," said k., and went up close to the supervisor. "i 
never meant it should be anything else," he replied. "it hardly seem 
to have been necessary to notify me of the arrest in that case," said k., 
and went even closer. the other had also come closer. all of them had 
gather together into a narrow space by the door. "that was my duty," 
said the supervisor. "a silly duty," said k., unyielding. "maybe so," 
reply the supervisor, "only don't let's waste our time talk on like 
this. i had assume you'd be wanting to go to the bank. as you're 
pay close attention to every word i'll add this: i'm not force you 
to go to the bank, i'd just assume you want to. and to make things 
easy for you, and to let you get to the bank with as little fuss as 
possible i've put these three gentlemen, colleague of yours, at your 
disposal." "what's that?" exclaim k., and look at the three in 
astonishment. he could only remember seeing them in their group by the 
photographs, but these characterless, anaemic young people were indeed 
official from his bank, not colleague of his, that was putt it too 
high and it show a gap in the omniscience of the supervisor, but they 
were nonetheless junior member of staff at the bank. how could k. have 
fail to see that? how occupy he must have been with the supervise 
and the policemen not to have recognise these three! rabensteiner, 
with his stiff demeanour and swinging hands, kullich, with his blonde 
hair and deep-set eyes, and kaminer, with his involuntary grin cause by 
chronic muscle spasms. "good morning," said k. after a while, extend 
his hand to the gentlemen as they bowed correct to him. "i didn't 
recognise you at all. so, we'll go into work now, shall we?" the 
gentlemen laugh and nod enthusiastically, as if that was what they 
had been wait for all the time, except that k. had left his hat in 
his room so they all dashed, one after another, into the room to fetch 
it, which cause a certain amount of embarrassment. k. stood where he 
was and watch them through the open double doorway, the last to go, of 
course, was the apathetic rabensteiner who had broken into no more than

that he in fact wasn't able to grin deliberately. at that moment mrs.

the people were. she did not seem to feel guilty about anything at all, 
and k., as often before, look down at the belt of her apron which, for 
no reason, cut so deep into her hefty body. once downstairs, k., with 
his watch in his hand, decided to take a taxi - he had already been 
delay by half an hour and there was no need to make the delay any

k. was cross with kullich for point out this man whom he had already

was there with his grin at k.'s service. it would have been inhumane to 
make fun of that. 

that spring, whenever possible, k. usually spent his evenings 
after work - he usually stay in the office until nine o'clock - with a 
short walk, either by himself or in the company of some of the bank 
officials, and then he would go into a pub where he would sit at the 
regulars' table with mostly old men until eleven. there were, 
however, also exception to this habit, times, for instance, when k. was 
invite by the bank's manager (whom he greatly respect for his 
industry and trustworthiness) to go with him for a ride in his car or to 
eat dinner with him at his large house. k. would also go, once a week, 
to see a girl call elsa who work as a waitress in a wine bar through 
the night until late in the morning. during the daytime she only 
received visitor while still in bed. 

that evening, though, - the day had pass quick with a lot of 
hard work and many respectful and friendly birthday greeting - k. 
want to go straight home. each time he had any small break from the 
day's work he considered, without knowing exactly what he had in mind, 
that mrs. grubach's flat seem to have been put into great disarray by

into order. once order had been restored, every trace of those event 
would have been erase and everything would take its previous course 
once more. in particular, there was nothing to fear from the three bank 
officials, they had immerse themselves back into their paperwork and 
there was no alteration to be seen in them. k. had call each of them, 
separate or all together, into his office that day for no other reason 
than to observe them; he was always satisfy and had always been able 
to let them go again. 

at half past nine that evening, when he arrive back in front of

"there's nothing stupid about what you've said, mrs. grubach, or

will she shake hand with me? the supervise didn't shake hands,

he did not feel at all like go to bed, so he decided to stay

chapter two 
first cross-examination 

k. was informed by telephone that there would be a small hearing 
concerning his case the following sunday. he was made aware that these 
cross examination would follow one another regularly, perhaps not every 
week but quite frequently. on the one hand it was in everyone's interest 
to bring proceedings quick to their conclusion, but on the other hand 
every aspect of the examination had to be carry out thorough 
without lasting too long because of the associate stress. for these 
reasons, it had been decided to hold a series of brief examination 
following on one after another. sunday had been chosen as the day for 
the hearing so that k. would not be disturbed in his professional work. 
it was assume that he would be in agreement with this, but if he wish 
for another date then, as far as possible, he would be accommodated. 
cross-examinations could even be held in the night, for instance, but k. 
would probably not be fresh enough at that time. anyway, as long as k. 
made no objection, the hearing would be left on sundays. it was a 
matter of course that he would have to appear without fail, there was 
probably no need to point this out to him. he would be given the number 
of the building where he was to present himself, which was in a street 
in a suburb well away from the city centre which k. had never been to 
before. 

once he had received this notice, k. hung up the receiver without 
give an answer; he had decided immediately to go there that sunday, it 
was certainly necessary, proceedings had begun and he had to face up to 
it, and this first examination would probably also be the last. he was 
still standing in thought by the telephone when he heard the voice of 
the deputy director behind him - he want to use the telephone but k. 
stood in his way. "bad news?" ask the deputy director casually, not 
in order to find anything out but just to get k. away from the device. 
"no, no," said k., he step to one side but did not go away entirely. 
the deputy director picked up the receiver and, as he wait for his 
connection, turn away from it and said to k., "one question, mr. k.: 
would you like to give me the pleasure of join me on my sailing boat 
on sunday morning? there's quite a few people coming, you're bound to 
know some of them. one of them is hasterer, the state attorney. would 
you like to come along? do come along!" k. try to pay attention to 
what the deputy director was saying. it was of no small importance for 
him, as this invitation from the deputy director, with whom he had never 
got on very well, meant that he was try to improve his relations with 
him. it show how important k. had become in the bank and how its 
second most important official seem to value his friendship, or at 
least his impartiality. he was only speak at the side of the 
telephone receiver while he wait for his connection, but in give 
this invitation the deputy director was humble himself. but k. would 
have to humiliate him a second time as a result, he said, "thank you 
very much, but i'm afraid i will have no time on sunday, i have a 
previous obligation." "pity," said the deputy director, and turn to 
the telephone conversation that had just been connected. it was not a 
short conversation, but k., remain standing confuse by the instrument 
all the time it was go on. it was only when the deputy director hung 
up that he was shock into aware and said, in order to partially 
excuse his standing there for no reason, "i've just received a telephone 
call, there's somewhere i need to go, but they forgot to tell me what 
time." "ask them then," said the deputy director. "it's not that 
important," said k., although in that way his early excuse, already 
weak enough, was made even weaker. as he went, the deputy director 
continue to speak about other things. k. forced himself to answer, but 
his thought were mainly about that sunday, how it would be best to get 
there for nine o'clock in the morning as that was the time that court 
always start work on weekdays. 

the weather was dull on sunday. k. was very tired, as he had 
stay out drink until late in the night celebrate with some of the 
regulars, and he had almost overslept. he dress hurriedly, without 
the time to think and assemble the various plan he had work out 
during the week. with no breakfast, he rush to the suburb he had been 
told about. oddly enough, although he had little time to look around 
him, he came across the three bank official involved in his case, 
rabensteiner, kullich and kaminer. the first two were travel in a 
tram that went across k.'s route, but kaminer sat on the terrace of a 
café and leant curious over the wall as k. came over. all of them 
seem to be look at him, surprise at seeing their superior running; 
it was a kind of pride that made k. want to go on foot, this was his 
affair and the idea of any help from strangers, however slight, was 
repulsive to him, he also want to avoid ask for anyone's help 
because that would initiate them into the affair even if only slightly. 
and after all, he had no wish at all to humiliate himself before the 
committee by being too punctual. anyway, now he was running so that he 
would get there by nine o'clock if at all possible, even though he had 
no appointment for this time. 

he had thought that he would recognise the building from a 
distance by some kind of sign, without knowing exactly what the sign 
would look like, or from some particular kind of activity outside the 
entrance. k. had been told that the building was in juliusstrasse, but 
when he stood at the street's entrance it consist on each side of 
almost nothing but monotonous, grey constructions, tall block of flat 
occupy by poor people. now, on a sunday morning, most of the window 
were occupied, men in their shirtsleeve leant out smoking, or careful 
and gently held small children on the sills. other window were pile 
up with bedding, above which the dishevelled head of a woman would 
brief appear. people call out to each other across the street, one 
of the call provoke a loud laugh about k. himself. it was a long 
street, and space even along it were small shop below street level, 
sell various kind of foodstuffs, which you reach by go down a 
few steps. women went in and out of them or stood chat on the 
steps. a fruitmonger, taking his goods up to the windows, was just as 
inattentive as k. and nearly knock him down with his cart. just then, 
a gramophone, which in better parts of town would have been seen as worn 
out, began to play some murderous tune. 

k. went further into the street, slowly, as if he had plenty of 
time now, or as if the examine magistrate were look at him from one 
of the window and therefore knew that k. had found his way there. it 
was shortly after nine. the building was quite far down the street, it 
cover so much area it was almost extraordinary, and the gateway in 
particular was tall and long. it was clearly intended for delivery 
wagon belong to the various warehouse all round the yard which were 
now lock up and carry the name of company some of which k. knew 
from his work at the bank. in contrast with his usual habits, he 
remain standing a while at the entrance to the yard taking in all 
these external details. near him, there was a bare-footed man sitting 
on a crate and reading a newspaper. there were two lad swinging on a 
hand cart. in front of a pump stood a weak, young girl in a bedjacket 
who, as the water flow into her can, look at k. there was a piece 
of rope stretch between two window in a corner of the yard, with some 
washing hanging on it to dry. a man stood below it calling out 
instruction to direct the work being done. 

k. went over to the stairway to get to the room where the hearing 
was to take place, but then stood still again as besides these steps he 
could see three other stairway entrances, and there also seem to be a 
small passageway at the end of the yard leading into a second yard. it 
irritate him that he had not been given more precise directions to the 
room, it meant they were either being especially neglectful with him or 
especially indifferent, and he decided to make that clear to them very 
loud and very unambiguously. in the end he decided to climb up the 
stairs, his thought play on something that he remember the 
policeman, willem, saying to him; that the court is attract by the 
guilt, from which it follow that the courtroom must be on the stairway 
that k. select by chance. 

as he went up he disturbed a large group of children play on 
the stairs who look at him as he step through their rows. "next 
time i come here," he said to himself, "i must either bring sweets with 
me to make them like me or a stick to hit them with." just before he 
reach the first landing he even had to wait a little while until a 
ball had finished its movement, two small lad with sly face like 
grown-up scoundrel held him by his trouser-legs until it had; if he 
were to shake them off he would have to hurt them, and he was afraid of 
what noise they would make by shouting. 

on the first floor, his search began for real. he still felt 
unable to ask for the investigate committee, and so he invent a 
joiner call lanz - that name occur to him because the captain, mrs. 
grubach's nephew, was call lanz - so that he could ask at every flat 
whether lanz the joiner live there and thus obtain a chance to look 
into the rooms. it turn out, though, that that was mostly possible 
without further ado, as almost all the door were left open and the 
children ran in and out. most of them were small, one-windowed rooms 
where they also did the cooking. many women held baby in one arm and 
work at the stove with the other. half grown girls, who seem to be 
dress in just their pinafore work hardest running to and fro. in 
every room, the bed were still in use by people who were ill, or still 
asleep, or people stretch out on them in their clothes. k. knock at 
the flat where the door were closed and ask whether lanz the joiner 
live there. it was usually a woman who open the door, heard the 
enquiry and turn to somebody in the room who would raise himself from 
the bed. "the gentleman's ask if a joiner call lanz, lives here." 
"a joiner, call lanz?" he would ask from the bed." "that's right," k. 
would say, although it was clear that the investigate committee was 
not to be found there, and so his task was at an end. there were many 
who thought it must be very important for k. to find lanz the joiner and 
thought long about it, name a joiner who was not call lanz or give 
a name that had some vague similarity with lanz, or they ask 
neighbour or accompany k. to a door a long way away where they 
thought someone of that sort might live in the back part of the building 
or where someone would be who could advise k. better than they could 
themselves. k. eventually had to give up ask if he did not want to 
be led all round from floor to floor in this way. he regret his 
initial plan, which had at first seem so practical to him. as he 
reach the fifth floor, he decided to give up the search, took his 
leave of a friendly, young worker who want to lead him on still 
further and went down the stairs. but then the thought of how much time 
he was waste made him cross, he went back again and knock at the 
first door on the fifth floor. the first thing he saw in the small room 
was a large clock on the wall which already show ten o'clock. "is 
there a joiner call lanz who lives here?" he asked. "pardon?" said a 
young woman with black, shining eyes who was, at that moment, washing 
children's underclothes in a bucket. she pointed her wet hand towards 
the open door of the adjoin room. 

k. thought he had step into a meeting. a medium sized, two 
window room was fill with the most diverse crowd of people - nobody 
paid any attention to the person who had just entered. close under its 
ceiling it was surround by a gallery which was also fully occupy and 
where the people could only stand bent down with their head and their 
back touching the ceiling. k., who found the air too stuffy, step 
out again and said to the young woman, who had probably misunderstood 
what he had said, "i ask for a joiner, someone by the name of lanz." 
"yes," said the woman, "please go on in." k. would probably not have 
follow her if the woman had not gone up to him, taken hold of the door 
handle and said, "i'll have to close the door after you, no-one else 
will be allow in." "very sensible," said k., "but it's too full 
already." but then he went back in anyway. he pass through between 
two men who were talk beside the door - one of them held both hand 
far out in front of himself making the movements of count out money, 
the other look him close in the eyes - and someone took him by the 
hand. it was a small, red-faced youth. "come in, come in," he said. 
k. let himself be led by him, and it turn out that there was - 
surprising in a dense packed crowd of people moving to and fro - a 
narrow passage which may have been the division between two factions; 
this idea was reinforce by the fact that in the first few row to the 
left and the right of him there was hardly any face look in his 
direction, he saw nothing but the back of people direct their speech 
and their movements only towards member of their own side. most of 
them were dress in black, in old, long, formal frock coat that hung 
down loose around them. these clothes were the only thing that 
puzzle k., as he would otherwise have taken the whole assembly for a 
local politics meeting. 

at the other end of the hall where k. had been led there was a 
little table set at an angle on a very low podium which was as 
overcrowd as everywhere else, and behind the table, near the edge of 
the podium, sat a small, fat, wheeze man who was talk with someone 
behind him. this second man was standing with his leg cross and his 
elbow on the backrest of the chair, provoking much laughter. from time 
to time he threw his arm in the air as if do a caricature of someone. 
the youth who was leading k. had some difficulty in report to the 
man. he had already try twice to tell him something, standing on tip- 
toe, but without get the man's attention as he sat there above him. 
it was only when one of the people up on the podium drew his attention 
to the youth that the man turn to him and leant down to hear what it 
was he quiet said. then he pull out his watch and quick look 
over at k. "you should have been here one hour and five minutes ago," 
he said. k. was go to give him a reply but had no time to do so, as 
hardly had the man spoken than a general mutter arose all over the 
right hand side of the hall. "you should have been here one hour and 
five minutes ago," the man now repeated, raise his voice this time, 
and quick look round the hall beneath him. the mutter also 
became immediately loud and, as the man said nothing more, die away 
only gradually. now the hall was much quiet than when k. had entered. 
only the people up in the gallery had not stop passing remarks. as 
far as could be distinguished, up in the half-darkness, dust and haze, 
they seem to be less well dress than those below. many of them had 
brought pillow that they had put between their head and the ceiling so 
that they would not hurt themselves pressed against it. 

k. had decided he would do more watch than talking, so he did 
not defend himself for supposedly have come late, and simply said, 
"well maybe i have arrive late, i'm here now." there follow loud 
applause, once more from the right hand side of the hall. easy people 
to get on your side, thought k., and was bother only by the quiet from 
the left hand side which was directly behind him and from which there 
was applause from only a few individuals. he wonder what he could say 
to get all of them to support him together or, if that were not 
possible, to at least get the support of the other for a while. 

"yes," said the man, "but i'm now no long under any obligation 
to hear your case" - there was once more a muttering, but this time it 
was mislead as the man wave the people's objection aside with his 
hand and continue - "i will, however, as an exception, continue with it 
today. but you should never arrive late like this again. and now, step 
forward!" someone jump down from the podium so that there would be a 
place free for k., and k. step up onto it. he stood pressed close 
against the table, the press of the crowd behind him was so great that 
he had to press back against it if he did not want to push the judge's 
desk down off the podium and perhaps the judge along with it. 

the judge, however, paid no attention to that but sat very 
comfortable on his chair and, after saying a few words to close his 
discussion with the man behind him, reach for a little note book, the 
only item on his desk. it was like an old school exercise book and had 
become quite misshapen from much thumbing. "now then," said the judge, 
thumb through the book. he turn to k. with the tone of someone who 
know his fact and said, "you are a house painter?" "no," said k., "i 
am the chief clerk in a large bank." this reply was follow by 
laughter among the right hand faction down in the hall, it was so hearty 
that k. couldn't stop himself join in with it. the people support 
themselves with their hand on their knee and shook as if suffering a 
serious attack of coughing. even some of those in the gallery were 
laughing. the judge had become quite cross but seem to have no power 
over those below him in the hall, he try to reduce what harm had been 
done in the gallery and jump up threaten them, his eyebrows, until 
then hardly remarkable, pushed themselves up and became big, black and 
bushy over his eyes. 

the left hand side of the hall was still quiet, though, the people 
stood there in row with their face look towards the podium 
listen to what was being said there, they observe the noise from the 
other side of the hall with the same quiet and even allow some 
individual from their own ranks, here and there, to go forward into the 
other faction. the people in the left faction were not only few in 
number than the right but probably were no more important than them, 
although their behaviour was calm and that made it seem like they 
were. when k. now began to speak he was convinced he was do it in 
the same way as them. 

"your question, my lord, as to whether i am a house painter - in 
fact even more than that, you did not ask at all but merely impose it 
on me - is symptomatic of the whole way these proceedings against me are 
being carry out. perhaps you will object that there are no 
proceedings against me. you will be quite right, as there are 
proceedings only if i acknowledge that there are. but, for the moment, 
i do acknowledge it, out of pity for yourself to a large extent. it's 
impossible not to observe all this business without feeling pity. i 
don't say things are being done without due care but i would like to 
make it clear that it is i who make the acknowledgement." 

k. stop speak and look down into the hall. he had spoken 
sharply, more sharp than he had intended, but he had been quite right. 
it should have been reward with some applause here and there but 
everything was quiet, they were all clearly wait for what would 
follow, perhaps the quiet was lay the ground for an outbreak of 
activity that would bring this whole affair to an end. it was somewhat 
disturb that just then the door at the end of the hall opened, the 
young washerwoman, who seem to have finished her work, came in and, 
despite all her caution, attract the attention of some of the people 
there. it was only the judge who gave k. any direct pleasure, as he 
seem to have been immediately struck by k.'s words. until then, he 
had listen to him standing, as k.'s speech had taken him by surprise 
while he was direct his attention to the gallery. now, in the pause, 
he sat down very slowly, as if he did not want anyone to notice. he 
took out the notebook again, probably so that he could give the 
impression of being calmer. 

"that won't help you, sir," continue k., "even your little book 
will only confirm what i say." k. was satisfy to hear nothing but his 
own quiet words in this room full of strangers, and he even dare 
casual to pick up the examine judge's notebook and, touching it only 
with the tip of his finger as if it were something revolting, lift 
it in the air, holding it just by one of the middle page so that the 
other on each side of it, close written, blot and yellowing, 
flap down. "those are the official note of the examine judge," he 
said, and let the notebook fall down onto the desk. "you can read in 
your book as much as you like, sir, i really don't have anything in this 
charge book to be afraid of, even though i don't have access to it as i 
wouldn't want it in my hand, i can only touch it with two fingers." the 
judge grab the notebook from where it had fallen on the desk - which 
could only have been a sign of his deep humiliation, or at least that is 
how it must have been perceive - try to tidy it up a little, and held 
it once more in front of himself in order to read from it. 

the people in the front row look up at him, showing such tension 
on their face that he look back down at them for some time. every 
one of them was an old man, some of them with white beards. could they 
perhaps be the crucial group who could turn the whole assembly one way 
or the other? they had sunk into a state of motionless while k. 
gave his oration, and it had not been possible to raise them from this 
passivity even when the judge was being humiliated. "what has happen 
to me," continue k., with less of the vigour he had had earlier, he 
continual scan the face in the first row, and this gave his 
address a somewhat nervous and distracted character, "what has happen 
to me is not just an isolated case. if it were it would not be of much 
importance as it's not of much importance to me, but it is a symptom of 
proceedings which are carry out against many. it's on behalf of them 
that i stand here now, not for myself alone." 

without have intended it, he had raise his voice. somewhere in 
the hall, someone raise his hand and applaud him shouting, "bravo! 
why not then? bravo! again i say, bravo!" some of the men in the 
first row grope around in their beards, none of them look round to 
see who was shouting. not even k. thought him of any importance but it 
did raise his spirits; he no long thought it at all necessary that all 
of those in the hall should applaud him, it was enough if the majority 
of them began to think about the matter and if only one of them, now 
and then, was persuaded. 

"i'm not try to be a successful orator," said k. after this 
thought, "that's probably more than i'm capable of anyway. i'm sure the 
examine judge can speak far better than i can, it is part of his job 
after all. all that i want is a public discussion of a public wrong. 
listen: ten days ago i was placed under arrest, the arrest itself is 
something i laugh about but that's beside the point. they came for me 
in the morning when i was still in bed. maybe the order had been given 
to arrest some house painter - that seem possible after what the judge 
has said - someone who is as innocent as i am, but it was me they chose. 
there were two police thug occupy the next room. they could not 
have taken better precaution if i had been a dangerous robber. and 
these policemen were unprincipled riff-raff, they talk at me till i 
was sick of it, they want bribes, they want to trick me into give 
them my clothes, they want money, supposedly so that they could bring 
me my breakfast after they had blatant eaten my own breakfast in front 
of my eyes. and even that was not enough. i was led in front of the 
supervise in another room. this was the room of a lady who i have a 
lot of respect for, and i was forced to look on while the supervise and 
the policemen made quite a mess of this room because of me, although not 
through any fault of mine. it was not easy to stay calm, but i manage 
to do so and was completely calm when i ask the supervise why it was 
that i was under arrest. if he were here he would have to confirm what 
i say. i can see him now, sitting on the chair belong to that lady i 
mention - a picture of dull-witted arrogance. what do you think he 
answered? what he told me, gentlemen, was basically nothing at all; 
perhaps he really did know nothing, he had placed me under arrest and 
was satisfied. in fact he had done more than that and brought three 
junior employee from the bank where i work into the lady's room; they 
had made themselves busy interfere with some photograph that belong 
to the lady and cause a mess. there was, of course, another reason 
for bring these employees; they, just like my landlady and her maid, 
were expect to spread the news of my arrest and damage my public 
reputation and in particular to remove me from my position at the bank. 
well they didn't succeed in any of that, not in the slightest, even my 
landlady, who is quite a simple person - and i will give you here her 
name in full respect, her name is mrs. grubach - even mrs. grubach was 
understanding enough to see that an arrest like this has no more 
significance than an attack carry out on the street by some youth who 
are not kept under proper control. i repeat, this whole affair has 
cause me nothing but unpleasant and temporary irritation, but could 
it not also have had some far worse consequences?" 

k. broke off here and look at the judge, who said nothing. as 
he did so he thought he saw the judge use a movement of his eyes to give 
a sign to someone in the crowd. k. smile and said, "and now the judge, 
right next to me, is give a secret sign to someone among you. there 
seem to be someone among you who is taking directions from above. i 
don't know whether the sign is meant to produce boo or applause, but 
i'll resist try to guess what its meaning is too soon. it really 
doesn't matter to me, and i give his lordship the judge my full and 
public permission to stop give secret sign to his paid subordinate 
down there and give his orders in words instead; let him just say "boo 
now!," and then the next time "clap now!". 

whether it was embarrassment or impatience, the judge rock 
backwards and forward on his seat. the man behind him, whom he had 
been talk with earlier, leant forward again, either to give him a few 
general words of encouragement or some specific piece of advice. below 
them in the hall the people talk to each other quiet but animatedly. 
the two faction had early seem to hold view strong oppose to 
each other but now they began to intermingle, a few individual pointed 
up at k., other pointed at the judge. the air in the room was fuggy 
and extremely oppressive, those who were standing furthest away could 
hardly even be seen through it. it must have been especially 
troublesome for those visitor who were in the gallery, as they were 
forced to quiet ask the participant in the assembly what exactly was 
happening, albeit with timid glance at the judge. the reply they 
received were just as quiet, and given behind the protection of a raise 
hand. 

"i have nearly finished what i have to say," said k., and as there 
was no bell available he struck the desk with his fist in a way that 
startle the judge and his advisor and made them look up from each 
other. "none of this concern me, and i am therefore able to make a calm 
assessment of it, and, assume that this so-called court is of any real 
importance, it will be very much to your advantage to listen to what i 
have to say. if you want to discuss what i say, please don't bother to 
write it down until later on, i don't have any time to waste and i'll 
soon be leaving." 

there was immediate silence, which show how well k. was in 
control of the crowd. there were no shout among them as there had been 
at the start, no-one even applauded, but if they weren't already 
persuade they seem very close to it. 

k was pleased at the tension among all the people there as they 
listen to him, a rustling rose from the silence which was more 
invigorate than the most ecstatic applause could have been. "there is 
no doubt," he said quietly, "that there is some enormous organisation 
determine what is said by this court. in my case this include my 
arrest and the examination taking place here today, an organisation that 
employ policemen who can be bribed, oafish supervise and judge of 
whom nothing better can be said than that they are not as arrogant as 
some others. this organisation even maintain a high-level judiciary 
along with its train of countless servants, scribes, policemen and all 
the other assistance that it needs, perhaps even executioner and 
torture - i'm not afraid of use those words. and what, gentlemen, is 
the purpose of this enormous organisation? its purpose is to arrest 
innocent people and wage pointless prosecution against them which, as 
in my case, lead to no result. how are we to avoid those in office 
becoming deep corrupt when everything is devoid of meaning? that is 
impossible, not even the highest judge would be able to achieve that for 
himself. that is why policemen try to steal the clothes off the back of 
those they arrest, that is why supervise break into the home of 
people they do not know, that is why innocent people are humiliate in 
front of crowd rather than being given a proper trial. the policemen 
only talk about the warehouse where they put the property of those 
they arrest, i would like to see these warehouse where the hard won 
possession of people under arrest is left to decay, if, that is, it's 
not stolen by the thieving hand of the warehouse workers." 

k. was interrupt by a screech from the far end of the hall, 
he shade his eyes to see that far, as the dull light of day made the 
smoke whitish and hard to see through. it was the washerwoman whom k. 
had recognise as a likely source of disturbance as soon as she had 
entered. it was hard to see now whether it was her fault or not. k. 
could only see that a man had pull her into a corner by the door and 
was pressing himself against her. but it was not her who was screaming, 
but the man, he had open his mouth wide and look up at the ceiling. 
a small circle had form around the two of them, the visitor near him 
in the gallery seem delight that the serious tone k. had introduce 
into the gathering had been disturbed in this way. k.'s first thought 
was to run over there, and he also thought that everyone would want to 
bring things back into order there or at least to make the pair leave 
the room, but the first row of people in front of him stay were they 
were, no-one move and no-one let k. through. on the contrary, they 
stood in his way, old men held out their arms in front of him and a hand 
from somewhere - he did not have the time to turn round - took hold of 
his collar. k., by this time, had forgotten about the pair, it seem 
to him that his freedom was being limited as if his arrest was being 
taken seriously, and, without any thought for what he was doing, he 
jump down from the podium. now he stood face to face with the crowd. 
had he judge the people properly? had he put too much faith in the 
effect of his speech? had they been putt up a pretence all the time 
he had been speaking, and now that he come to the end and to what must 
follow, were they tired of pretending? what face they were, all around 
him! dark, little eyes flicker here and there, cheek droop down 
like on drunken men, their long beard were thin and stiff, if they took 
hold of them it was more like they were making their hand into claws, 
not as if they were taking hold of their own beards. but underneath 
those beard - and this was the real discovery made by k. - there were 
badge of various size and colours shining on the collar of their 
coats. as far as he could see, every one of them was wearing one of 
these badges. all of them belong to the same group, even though they 
seem to be divide to the right and the left of him, and when he 
sudden turn round he saw the same badge on the collar of the 
examine judge who calm look down at him with his hand in his lap. 
"so," call out k, throw his arms in the air as if this sudden 
realisation need more room, "all of you are working for this 
organisation, i see now that you are all the very bunch of cheat and 
liar i've just been speak about, you've all pressed yourself in 
here in order to listen in and snoop on me, you gave the impression of 
have form into factions, one of you even applaud me to test me 
out, and you want to learn how to trap an innocent man! well, i hope 
you haven't come here for nothing, i hope you've either had some fun 
from someone who expect you to defend his innocence or else - let go 
of me or i'll hit you," shout k. to a quivery old man who had pressed

chapter four 
miss bürstner's friend 

for some time after this, k. found it impossible to exchange even 
just a few words with miss bürstner. he try to reach her in many and 
various ways but she always found a way to avoid it. he would come 
straight home from the office, remain in her room without the light on, 
and sit on the sofa with nothing more to distract him than keeping watch 
on the empty hallway. if the maid went by and closed the door of the 
apparently empty room he would get up after a while and open it again. 
he got up an hour early than usual in the morning so that he might 
perhaps find miss bürstner alone as she went to the office. but none of 
these effort brought any success. then he wrote her a letter, both to

offer to make whatever amends he could, promise never to cross 
whatever boundary she might set him and beg merely to have the chance

stipulation she might make. the letters were not returned, but there

could be seen shuffle through the hallway for several hours, there was

your reason for ask for this meeting - or at least i suppose she is
