would be of no benefit to anyone if this meeting actually took place. 
moreover, it was only yesterday, and only very briefly, that she made it 
clear to me that such a meeting could be of no benefit for yourself 
either, she feel that it can only have been a matter of chance that 
such an idea came to you, and that even without any explanation from 
her, you will very soon come to realise yourself, if you have not done 
so already, the futility of your idea. my answer to that is that 
although it may be quite right, i consider it advantageous, if the 
matter is to be made perfectly clear, to give you an explicit answer. i 
offer my service in taking on the task, and after some hesitation my 
friend conceded. i hope, however, also to have act in your interests, 
as even the slightest uncertainty in the least significant of matter 
will always remain a cause of suffering and if, as in this case, it can 
be remove without substantial effort, then it is better if that is done 
without delay." "i thank you," said k. as soon as miss montag had 
finished. he stood slow up, look at her, then across the table, 
then out the window - the house opposite stood there in the sun - and 
went to the door. miss montag follow him a few paces, as if she did 
not quite trust him. at the door, however, both of them had to step 
back as it open and captain lanz entered. this was the first time 
that k. had seen him close up. he was a large man of about forty with a 
tanned, fleshy face. he bowed slightly, intend it also for k., and 
then went over to miss montag and deferential kiss her hand. he was 
very elegant in the way he moved. the courtesy he show towards miss 
montag made a striking contrast with the way she had been treat by k. 
nonetheless, miss montag did not seem to be cross with k. as it even 
seem to him that she want to introduce the captain. k. however, did 
not want to be introduced, he would not have been able to show any sort 
of friendly either to miss montag or to the captain, the kiss on the 
hand had, for k., bound them into a group which would keep him at a 
distance from miss bürstner whilst at the same time seeming to be 
total harmless and unselfish. k. thought, however, that he saw more 
than that, he thought he also saw that miss montag had chosen a means of 
do it that was good, but two-edged. she exaggerate the importance 
of the relationship between k. and miss bürstner, and above all she 
exaggerate the importance of ask to speak with her and she try at 
the same time to make out that k. was exaggerate everything. she 
would be disappointed, k. did not want to exaggerate anything, he was 
aware that miss bürstner was a little typist who would not offer him 
much resistance for long. in do so he deliberate took no account 
of what mrs. grubach had told him about miss bürstner. all these things 
were go through his mind as he left the room with hardly a polite 
word. he want to go straight to his room, but a little laugh from 
miss montag that he heard from the dine room behind him brought him to 
the idea that he might prepare a surprise for the two of them, the 
captain and miss montag. he look round and listen to find out if 
there might be any disturbance from any of the surrounding rooms, 
everywhere was quiet, the only thing to be heard was the conversation 
from the dine room and mrs. grubach's voice from the passage leading 
to the kitchen. this seem an opportune time, k. went to miss 
bürstner's room and knock gently. there was no sound so he knock 
again but there was still no answer in reply. was she asleep? or was 
she really unwell? or was she just pretend as she realise it could 
only be k. knock so gently? k. assume she was pretend and 
knock harder, eventually, when the knock brought no result, he 
careful open the door with the sense of do something that was not 
only improper but also pointless. in the room there was no-one. what's 
more, it look hardly at all like the room k. had known before. 
against the wall there were now two bed behind one another, there were 
clothes pile up on three chair near the door, a wardrobe stood open. 
miss bürstner must have gone out while miss montag was speak to him 
in the dine room. k. was not greatly bother by this, he had hardly 
expect to be able to find miss bürstner so easily and had made this 
attempt for little more reason than to spite miss montag. but that made 
it all the more embarrass for him when, as he was close the door 
again, he saw miss montag and the captain talk in the open doorway of 
the dine room. they had probably been standing there ever since k. 
had open the door, they avoid seeming to observe k. but chat 
lightly and follow his movements with glances, the absent minded 
glance to the side such as you make during a conversation. but these 
glance were heavy for k., and he rush alongside the wall back into 
his own room. 




chapter five 
the whip-man 

one evening, a few days later, k. was walking along one of the 
corridor that separate his office from the main stairway - he was 
nearly the last one to leave for home that evening, there remain only 
a couple of worker in the light of a single bulb in the dispatch 
department - when he heard a sigh from behind a door which he had 
himself never open but which he had always thought just led into a 
junk room. he stood in amaze and listen again to establish 
whether he might not be mistaken. for a while there was silence, but 
then came some more sighs. his first thought was to fetch one of the 
servitors, it might well have been worth have a witness present, but 
then he was taken by an uncontrollable curiosity that make him simply 
yank the door open. it was, as he had thought, a junk room. old, 
unusable forms, empty stone ink-bottles lay scattered behind the 
entrance. but in the cupboard-like room itself stood three men, 
crouch under the low ceiling. a candle fixed on a shelf gave them 
light. "what are you do here?" ask k. quietly, but cross and 
without thinking. one of the men was clearly in charge, and attract 
attention by being dress in a kind of dark leather costume which left 
his neck and chest and his arms exposed. he did not answer. but the 
other two call out, "mr. k.! we're to be beaten because you made a 
complaint about us to the examine judge." and now, k. finally 
realise that it was actually the two policemen, franz and willem, and 
that the third man held a cane in his hand with which to beat them. 
"well," said k., staring at them, "i didn't make any complaint, i only 
said what took place in my home. and your behaviour was not entire 
unobjectionable, after all." "mr. k.," said willem, while franz clearly 
try to shelter behind him as protection from the third man, "if you 
knew how badly we get paid you wouldn't think so badly of us. i've got 
a family to feed, and franz here want to get married, you just have to 
get more money where you can, you can't do it just by working hard, not 
however hard you try. i was sorely tempt by your fine clothes, 
policemen aren't allow to do that sort of thing, course they aren't, 
and it wasn't right of us, but it's tradition that the clothes go to the 
officers, that's how it's always been, believe me; and it's 
understand too, isn't it, what can things like that mean for anyone 
unlucky enough to be arrested? but if he start talk about it openly 
then the punishment has to follow." "i didn't know about any of this 
that you've been telling me, and i made no sort of request that you be 
punished, i was simply acting on principle." "franz," said willem, 
turning to the other policeman, "didn't i tell you that the gentleman 
didn't say he want us to be punished? now you can hear for yourself, 
he didn't even know we'd have to be punished." "don't you let them 
persuade you, talk like that," said the third man to k., "this 
punishment is both just and unavoidable." "don't listen to him," said 
willem, interrupt himself only to quick bring his hand to his mouth 
when it had received a stroke of the cane, "we're only being punish 
because you made a complaint against us. nothing would have happen to 
us otherwise, not even if they'd found out what we'd done. can you call 
that justice? both of us, me especially, we'd prove our worth as good 
police officer over a long period - you've got to admit yourself that 
as far as official work was concerned we did the job well - things 
look good for us, we had prospects, it's quite certain that we 
would've been made whip-men too, like this one, only he had the luck not 
to have anyone make a complaint about him, as you really don't get many 
complaint like that. only that's all finished now, mr. k., our career 
are at an end, we're go to have to do work now that's far inferior to 
police work and besides all this we're go to get this terrible, 
painful beating." "can the cane really cause so much pain, then?" ask 
k., test the cane that the whip-man swang in front of him. "we're 
go to have to strip off total naked," said willem. "oh, i see," 
said k., look straight at the whip-man, his skin was burn brown 
like a sailor's, and his face show health and vigour. "is there 
then no possibility of sparing these two their beating?" he ask him. 
"no," said the whip-man, shake his head with a laugh. "get 
undressed!" he ordered the policemen. and to k. he said, "you shouldn't 
believe everything they tell you, it's the fear of being beaten, it's 
already made them a bit weak in the head. this one here, for instance," 
he pointed at willem, "all that he told you about his career prospects, 
it's just ridiculous. look at him, look how fat he is - the first 
stroke of the cane will just get lost in all that fat. do you know 
what it is that's made him so fat? he's in the habit of, everyone that 
get arrest by him, he eats their breakfast. didn't he eat up your 
breakfast? yeah, i thought as much. but a man with a belly like that 
can't be made into a whip-man and never will be, that is quite out of 
the question." "there are whip-men like that," willem insisted, who had 
just release the belt of this trousers. "no," said the whip-man, 
striking him such a blow with the cane on his neck that it made him 
wince, "you shouldn't be listen to this, just get undressed." "i 
would make it well worth your while if you would let them go," said k., 
and without look at the whip-man again - as such matter are best 
carry on with both pair of eyes turn down - he pull out his 
wallet. "and then you'd try and put in a complaint against me, too," 
said the whip-man, "and get me flogged. no, no!" "now, do be 
reasonable," said k., "if i had want to get these two punish i would 
not now be try to buy their freedom, would i. i could simply close 
the door here behind me, go home and see or hear nothing more of it. 
but that's not what i'm doing, it really is of much more importance to 
me to let them go free; if i had realise they would be punished, or 
even that they might be punished, i would never have name them in the 
first place as they are not the one i hold responsible. it's the 
organisation that's to blame, the high official are the one to blame." 
"that's how it is!" shout the policemen, who then immediately received 
another blow on their backs, which were by now exposed. "if you had a 
senior judge here beneath your stick," said k., pressing down the cane 
as he spoke to stop it being raise once more, "i really would do 
nothing to stop you, on the contrary, i would even pay you money to give 
you all the more strength." "yeah, that's all very plausible, what 
you're saying there," said the whip-man, "only i'm not the sort of 
person you can bribe. it's my job to flog people, so i flog them." 
franz, the policeman, had been fairly quiet so far, probably in 
expectation of a good result from k.'s intervention, but now he step 
forward to the door wearing just his trousers, kneel down hanging on 
to k.'s arm and whispered, "even if you can't get mercy shown for both 
of us, at least try and get me set free. willem is old than me, he's 
less sensitive than me in every way, he even got a light beating a 
couple of years ago, but my record's still clean, i only did things the 
way i did because willem led me on to it, he's been my teacher both for 
good and bad. down in front of the bank my poor bride is wait for me 
at the entrance, i'm so ashamed of myself, it's pitiful." his face was 
flowing over with tears, and he wipe it dry on k.'s coat. "i'm not 
go to wait any longer," said the whip-man, taking hold of the cane in 
both hand and lay in to franz while willem cower back in a corner 
and look on secretly, not even daring to turn his head. then, the 
sudden scream that shot out from franz was long and irrevocable, it 
seem to come not from a human being but from an instrument that was 
being tortured, the whole corridor rang with it, it must have been heard 
by everyone in the building. "don't shout like that!", call out k., 
unable to prevent himself, and, as he look anxious in the direction 
from which the servitor would come, he gave franz a shove, not hard, but 
hard enough for him to fall down unconscious, claw at the ground with 
his hand by reflex; he still did not avoid being hit; the rod still 
found him on the floor; the tip of the rod swang regularly up and down 
while he roll to and fro under its blows. and now one of the 
servitor appear in the distance, with another a few steps behind him. 
k. had quick thrown the door shut, gone over to one of the window 
overlook the yard and open it. the scream had completely stopped. 
so that the servitor wouldn't come in, he call out, "it's only me!" 
"good evening, chief clerk," somebody call back. "is there anything 
wrong?" "no, no," answer k., "it's only a dog yelp in the yard." 
there was no sound from the servitor so he added, "you can go back to 
what you were doing." he did not want to become involved with a 
conversation with them, and so he leant out of the window. a little 
while later, when he look out in the corridor, they had already gone. 
now, k. remain at the window, he did not dare go back into the junk 
room, and he did not want to go home either. the yard he look down 
into was small and rectangular, all around it were offices, all the 
window were now dark and only those at the very top caught a reflection 
of the moon. k try hard to see into the dark of one corner of the 
yard, where a few handcart had been left behind one another. he felt 
anguish at not have been able to prevent the flogging, but that was 
not his fault, if franz had not scream like that - clearly it must 
have cause a great deal of pain but it's important to maintain control 
of oneself at important moments - if franz had not scream then it was 
at least highly probable that k. would have been able to dissuade the 
whip-man. if all the junior officer were contemptible why would the 
whip-man, whose position was the most inhumane of all, be any exception, 
and k. had notice very clearly how his eyes had lit up when he saw the 
banknotes, he had obviously only seem serious about the flogging to 
raise the level of the bribe a little. and k. had not been ungenerous, 
he really had want to get the policemen freed; if he really had now 
begun to do something against the degeneracy of the court then it was a 
matter of course that he would have to do something here as well. but 
of course, it became impossible for him to do anything as soon as franz 
start screaming. k. could not possibly have let the junior bank 
staff, and perhaps even all sort of other people, come along and catch 
him by surprise as he haggle with those people in the junk room. 
nobody could really expect that sort of sacrifice of him. if that had 
been his intention then it would almost have been easier, k. would have 
taken his own clothes off and offer himself to the whip-man in the 
policemen's place. the whip-man would certainly not have accept this 
substitute anyway, as in that way he would have seriously violate his 
duty without gain any benefit. he would most likely have violate 
his duty twice over, as court employee were probably under orders not 
to cause any harm to k. while he was facing charges, although there may 
have been special conditions in force here. however things stood, k. 
was able to do no more than throw the door shut, even though that would 
still do nothing to remove all the danger he faced. it was regrettable 
that he had given franz a shove, and it could only be excuse by the 
heat of the moment. 

in the distance, he heard the steps of the servitors; he did not 
want them to be too aware of his presence, so he closed the window and 
walk towards the main staircase. at the door of the junk room he 
stop and listen for a little while. all was silent. the two 
policemen were entire at the whip-man's mercy; he could have beaten 
them to death. k. reach his hand out for the door handle but drew it 
sudden back. he was no long in any position to help anyone, and the 
servitor would soon be back; he did, though, promise himself that he 
would raise the matter again with somebody and see that, as far as it 
was in his power, those who really were guilty, the high official whom 
nobody had so far dare point out to him, received their due punishment. 
as he went down the main stairway at the front of the bank, he look 
careful round at everyone who was passing, but there was no girl to be 
seen who might have been wait for somebody, not even within some 
distance from the bank. franz's claim that his bride was wait for 
him was thus shown to be a lie, albeit one that was forgivable and 
intended only to elicit more sympathy. 

the policemen were still on k.'s mind all through the following 
day; he was unable to concentrate on his work and had to stay in his 
office a little long than the previous day so that he could finish it. 
on the way home, as he pass by the junk room again, he open its door 
as if that had been his habit. instead of the dark he expected, he 
saw everything unchanged from the previous evening, and did not know how 
he should respond. everything was exactly the same as he had seen it 
when he had open the door the previous evening. the form and 
bottle of ink just inside the doorway, the whip-man with his cane, the 
two policemen, still undressed, the candle on the shelf, and the two 
policemen began to wail and call out "mr. k.!" k. slam the door 
immediately shut, and even thump on it with his fist as if that would 
shut it all the firmer. almost in tears, he ran to the servitor 
working quiet at the copy machine. "go and get that junk room 
clear out!" he shouted, and, in amazement, they stop what they were 
doing. "it should have been done long ago, we're sink in dirt!" they 
would be able to do the job the next day, k. nodded, it was too late in 
the evening to make them do it there and then as he had originally 
intended. he sat down brief in order to keep them near him for a 
little longer, look through a few of the copy to give the impression 
that he was check them and then, as he saw that they would not dare 
to leave at the same time as himself, went home tired and with his mind 
numb. 




chapter six 
k.'s uncle - leni 

one afternoon - k. was very busy at the time, get the post 
ready - k.'s uncle karl, a small country land owner, came into the room, 
push his way between two of the staff who were bring in some 
papers. k. had long expect his uncle to appear, but the sight of him 
now shock k. far less than the prospect of it had done a long time 
before. his uncle was bound to come, k. had been sure of that for about 
a month. he already thought at the time he could see how his uncle 
would arrive, slightly bowed, his batter panama hat in his left hand, 
his right hand already stretch out over the desk long before he was 
close enough as he rush careless towards k. knock over everything 
that was in his way. k.'s uncle was always in a hurry, as he suffer 
from the unfortunate belief that he had a number of things to do while he 
was in the big city and had to settle all of them in one day - his 
visit were only ever for one day - and at the same time thought he 
could not forgo any conversation or piece of business or pleasure that 
might arise by chance. uncle karl was k.'s former guardian, and so k. 
was duty-bound to help him in all of this as well as to offer him a bed 
for the night. 'i'm haunt by a ghost from the country', he would say. 

as soon as they had greet each other - k. had invite him to sit 
in the armchair but uncle karl had no time for that - he said he want 
to speak brief with k. in private. "it is necessary," he said with a 
tired gulp, "it is necessary for my peace of mind." k. immediately 
sent the junior staff from the room and told them to let no-one in. 
"what's this that i've been hearing, josef?" cry k.'s uncle when they

without look at them to make himself more comfortable. k. said

"that's right," answer his uncle with a slow nod, "i've heard about

try to find an excuse, don't try to hide anything, no, you're quite open 
about it, you run off with her and stay there. and meanwhile we're 
sitting there, your uncle who's go to such effort for you, the lawyer 
who needs to be won over to your side, and above all the office 
director, a very important gentleman who is in direct command of your 
affair in its present stage. we want to discuss how best to help you, 
i had to handle the lawyer very carefully, he had to handle the office 
director carefully, and you had most reason of all to at least give me 
some support. instead of which you stay away. eventually we couldn't 
keep up the pretence any longer, but these are polite and highly capable 
men, they didn't say anything about it so as to spare my feelings but in 
the end not even they could continue to force themselves and, as they 
couldn't speak about the matter in hand, they became silent. we sat 
there for several minutes, listen to see whether you wouldn't finally 
come back. all in vain. in the end the office director stood up, as he 
had stay far long than he had originally intended, made his 
farewell, look at me in sympathy without being able to help, he wait 
at the door for a long time although it's more than i can understand why 
he was being so good, and then he went. i, of course, was glad he'd 
gone, i'd been holding my breath all this time. all this had even more 
affect on the lawyer lie there ill, when i took my leave of him, the 
good man, he was quite unable to speak. you have probably contribute 
to his total collapse and so brought the very man who you are dependent 
on close to his death. and me, your own uncle, you leave me here in 
the rain - just feel this, i'm wet right through - wait here for 
hours, sick with worry." 




chapter seven 
lawyer - manufacturer - painter 

one winter morning - snow was fall in the dull light outside - 
k. was sitting in his office, already extremely tired despite the early 
hour. he had told the servitor he was engaged in a major piece of work 
and none of the junior staff should be allow in to see him, so he 
would not be disturbed by them at least. but instead of working he 
turn round in his chair, slow move various item around his desk, 
but then, without being aware of it, he lay his arm stretch out on the 
desk top and sat there immobile with his head sunk down on his chest. 

he was no long able to get the thought of the trial out of his 
head. he had often wonder whether it might not be a good idea to work 
out a written defence and hand it in to the court. it would contain a 
short description of his life and explain why he had act the way he 
had at each event that was in any way important, whether he now 
considered he had act well or ill, and his reason for each. there 
was no doubt of the advantage a written defence of this sort would have 
over rely on the lawyer, who was anyway not without his shortcomings. 
k. had no idea what action the lawyer was taking; it was certainly not 
a lot, it was more than a month since the lawyer had summon him, and 
none of the previous discussion had given k. the impression that this 
man would be able to do much for him. most importantly, he had ask 
him hardly any questions. and there were so many question here to be 
asked. ask question were the most important thing. k. had the 
feeling that he would be able to ask all the question need here 
himself. the lawyer, in contrast, did not ask question but did all the 
talk himself or sat silent facing him, leant forward slightly over 
the desk, probably because he was hard of hearing, pull on a strand of 
hair in the middle of his beard and look down at the carpet, perhaps 
at the very spot where k. had lain with leni. now and then he would 
give k. some vague warning of the sort you give to children. his 
speech were as pointless as they were boring, and k. decided that when 
the final bill came he would pay not a penny for them. once the lawyer 
thought he had humiliate k. sufficiently, he usually start something 
that would raise his spirits again. he had already, he would then say, 
won many such cases, partly or in whole, case which may not really have 
been as difficult as this one but which, on the face of it, had even 
less hope of success. he had a list of these case here in the drawer - 
here he would tap on one or other of the drawers in his desk - but 
could, unfortunately, not show them to k. as they dealt with official 
secrets. nonetheless, the great experience he had acquire through all 
these case would, of course, be of benefit to k. he had, of course, 
begun work straight away and was nearly ready to submit the first 
documents. they would be very important because the first impression 
made by the defence will often determine the whole course of the 
proceedings. unfortunately, though, he would still have to make it 
clear to k. that the first document submit are sometimes not even 
read by the court. they simply put them with the other document and 
point out that, for the time being, questioning and observing the 
accused are much more important than anything written. if the applicant 
become insistent, then they add that before they come to any decision, 
as soon as all the material has been brought together, with due regard, 
of course, to all the documents, then these first document to have been 
submit will also be checked over. but unfortunately, even this is 
not usually true, the first document submit are usually mislaid or 
lost completely, and even if they do keep them right to the end they are 
hardly read, although the lawyer only knew about this from rumour. this 
is all very regrettable, but not entire without its justifications. 
but k. should not forget that the trial would not be public, if the 
court deem it necessary it can be made public but there is no law that 
say it has to be. as a result, the accused and his defence don't have 
access even to the court records, and especially not to the indictment, 
and that means we generally don't know - or at least not precisely - 
what the first document need to be about, which means that if they do 
contain anything of relevance to the case it's only by a lucky 
coincidence. if anything about the individual charge and the reason 
for them come out clearly or can be guess at while the accused is 
being questioned, then it's possible to work out and submit document 
that really direct the issue and present proof, but not before. 
conditions like this, of course, place the defence in a very 
unfavourable and difficult position. but that is what they intend. in 
fact, defence is not really allow under the law, it's only tolerated, 
and there is even some dispute about whether the relevant parts of the 
law imply even that. so strict speaking, there is no such thing as a 
counsel acknowledge by the court, and anyone who come before this 
court as counsel is basically no more than a barrack room lawyer. the 
effect of all this, of course, is to remove the dignity of the whole 
procedure, the next time k. is in the court offices he might like to 
have a look in at the lawyers' room, just so that he's seen it. he 
might well be quite shock by the people he see assemble there. the 
room they've been allocated, with its narrow space and low ceiling, will 
be enough to show what contempt the court has for these people. the 
only light in the room come through a little window that is so high up 
that, if you want to look out of it, you first have to get one of your 
colleague to support you on his back, and even then the smoke from the 
chimney just in front of it will go up your nose and make your face 
black. in the floor of this room - to give yet another example of the 
conditions there - there is a hole that's been there for more than a 
year, it's not so big that a man could fall through, but it is big 
enough for your foot to disappear through it. the lawyers' room is on 
the second floor of the attic; if your foot do go through it will hang 
down into the first floor of the attic underneath it, and right in the 
corridor where the litigant are waiting. it's no exaggeration when 
lawyer say that conditions like that are a disgrace. complaint to the 
management don't have the slightest effect, but the lawyer are strict 
forbidden to alter anything in the room at their own expense. but even 
treat the lawyer in this way has its reasons. they want, as far as 
possible, to prevent any kind of defence, everything should be made the 
responsibility of the accused. not a bad point of view, basically, but 
nothing could be more mistaken than to think from that that lawyer are 
not necessary for the accused in this court. on the contrary, there is 
no court where they are less need than here. this is because 
proceedings are generally kept secret not only from the public but also 
from the accused. only as far as that is possible, of course, but it is 
possible to a very large extent. and the accused doesn't get to see the 
court record either, and it's very difficult to infer what's in the 
court record from what's been said during questioning base on them, 
especially for the accused who is in a difficult situation and is face 
with every possible worry to distract him. this is when the defence 
begins. counsel for the defence are not normally allow to be present 
while the accused is being questioned, so afterwards, and if possible 
still at the door of the interview room, he has to learn what he can 
about it from him and extract whatever he can that might be of use, even 
though what the accused has to report is often very confused. but that 
is not the most important thing, as there's really not a lot that can be 
learned in this way, although in this, as with anything else, a 
competent man will learn more than another. nonetheless, the most 
important thing is the lawyer's personal connections, that's where the 
real value of taking counsel lies. now k. will most likely have already 
learned from his own experience that, among its very lowest orders, the 
court organisation do have its imperfections, the court is strict 
closed to the public, but staff who forget their duty or who take bribe 
do, to some extent, show where the gap are. this is where most lawyer 
will push their way in, this is where bribe are paid and information 
extracted, there have even, in early times at least, been incident 
where document have been stolen. there's no deny that some 
surprising favourable result have been attain for the accused in 
this way, for a limited time, and these petty advocate then strut to 
and fro on the basis of them and attract new clients, but for the 
further course of the proceedings it signify either nothing or nothing 
good. the only things of real value are honest personal contacts, 
contact with higher officials, albeit higher official of the lower 
grades, you understand. that is the only way the progress of the trial 
can be influenced, hardly noticeable at first, it's true, but from then 
on it become more and more visible. there are, of course, not many 
lawyer who can do this, and k. has made a very good choice in this 
matter. there were probably no more than one or two who had as many 
contact as dr. huld, but they don't bother with the company of the 
lawyers' room and have nothing to do with it. this means they have all 
the less contact with the court officials. it is not at all necessary 
for dr. huld to go to the court, wait in the ante-rooms for the 
examine judge to turn up, if they turn up, and try to achieve 
something which, according to the judges' mood is usually more apparent 
than real and most often not even that. no, k. has seen for himself 
that the court officials, including some who are quite high up, come 
forward without being asked, are glad to give information which is fully 
open or at least easy to understand, they discuss the next stage in the 
proceedings, in fact in some case they can be won over and are quite 
willing to adopt the other person's point of view. however, when this 
happens, you should never trust them too far, as however firm they may 
have declared this new point of view in favour of the defendant they 
might well go straight back to their offices and write a report for the 
court that say just the opposite, and might well be even hard on the 
defendant than the original view, the one they insist they've been fully 
dissuade from. and, of course, there's no way of defend yourself 
from this, something said in private is indeed in private and cannot 
then be use in public, it's not something that make it easy for the 
defence to keep those gentlemen's favour. on the other hand, it's also 
true that the gentlemen don't become involved with the defence - which 
will of course be done with great expertise - just for philanthropic 
reason or in order to be friendly, in some respects it would be true 
to say that they, too, have it allocate to them. this is where the 
disadvantage of a court structure that, right from the start, 
stipulate that all proceedings take place in private, come into force. 
in normal, mediocre trial its official have contact with the public, 
and they're very well equip for it, but here they don't; normal 
trial run their course all by themselves, almost, and just need a nudge 
here and there; but when they're face with case that are especially 
difficult they're as lost as they often are with one that are very 
simple; they're forced to spend all their time, day and night, with 
their laws, and so they don't have the right feel for human 
relationships, and that's a serious shortcoming in case like this. 
that's when they come for advice to the lawyer, with a servant behind 
them carry the document which normally are kept so secret. you 
could have seen many gentlemen at this window, gentlemen of whom you 
would least expect it, staring out this window in despair on the street 
below while the lawyer is at his desk study the document so that he 
can give them good advice. and at times like that it's also possible to 
see how exceptional seriously these gentlemen take their profession 
and how they are thrown into great confusion by difficulty which it's 
just not in their nature to overcome. but they're not in an easy 
position, to regard their position as easy would be to do them an 
injustice. the different ranks and hierarchy of the court are 
endless, and even someone who know his way around them cannot always 
tell what's go to happen. but even for the junior officials, the 
proceedings in the courtroom are usually kept secret, so they are 
hardly able to see how the case they work with proceed, court affair 
appear in their range of vision often without their knowing where they 
come from and they move on further without their learning where they go. 
so civil servant like this are not able to learn the things you can 
learn from study the successive stage that individual trial go 
through, the final verdict or the reason for it. they're only allow 
to deal with that part of the trial which the law allocate them, and 
they usually know less about the result of their work after it's left 
them than the defence does, even though the defence will usually stay in 
contact with the accused until the trial is nearly at its end, so that 
the court official can learn many useful things from the defence. 
bearing all this in mind, do it still surprise k. that the official 
are irritate and often express themselves about the litigant in 
unflattere ways - which is an experience share by everyone. all the 
official are irritated, even when they appear calm. this cause many 
difficulty for the junior advocates, of course. there is a story, for 
instance, that has very much the ring of truth about it. it go like 
this: one of the old officials, a good and peaceful man, was dealing 
with a difficult matter for the court which had become very confused, 
especially thanks to the contribution from the lawyers. he had been 
study it for a day and a night without a break - as these official 
are indeed hard working, no-one works as hard as they do. when it was 
nearly morning, and he had been working for twenty-four hour with 
probably very little result, he went to the front entrance, wait there 
in ambush, and every time a lawyer try to enter the building he would 
throw him down the steps. the lawyer gather together down in front 
of the steps and discuss with each other what they should do; on the 
one hand they had actually no right to be allow into the building so 
that there was hardly anything that they could legal do to the 
official and, as i've already mentioned, they would have to be careful 
not to set all the official against them. on the other hand, any day 
not spent in court is a day lost for them and it was a matter of some 
importance to force their way inside. in the end, they agree that they 
would try to tire the old man out. one lawyer after another was sent 
out to run up the steps and let himself be thrown down again, offering 
what resistance he could as long as it was passive resistance, and his 
colleague would catch him at the bottom of the steps. that went on for 
about an hour until the old gentleman, who was already exhaust from 
working all night, was very tired and went back to his office. those 
who were at the bottom of the steps could not believe it at first, so 
they sent somebody out to go and look behind the door to see if there 
really was no-one there, and only then did they all gather together and 
probably didn't even dare to complain, as it's far from being the 
lawyers' job to introduce any improvement in the court system, or even 
to want to. even the most junior lawyer can understand the relationship 
there to some extent, but one significant point is that almost every 
defendant, even very simple people, begin to think of suggestion for 
improve the court as soon as his proceedings have begun, many of them 
often even spend time and energy on the matter that could be spent far 
better elsewhere. the only right thing to do is to learn how to deal 
with the situation as it is. even if it were possible to improve any 
detail of it - which is anyway no more than superstitious nonsense - the 
best that they could achieve, although do themselves incalculable 
harm in the process, is that they will have attract the special 
attention of the official for any case that come up in the future, and 
the official are always ready to seek revenge. never attract attention 
to yourself! stay calm, however much it go against your character! 
try to gain some insight into the size of the court organism and how, to 
some extent, it remains in a state of suspension, and that even if you 
alter something in one place you'll draw the ground out from under your 
feet and might fall, whereas if an enormous organism like the court is 
disrupt in any one place it find it easy to provide a substitute for 
itself somewhere else. everything is connected with everything else and 
will continue without any change or else, which is quite probable, even 
more closed, more attentive, more strict, more malevolent. so it's best 
to leave the work to the lawyer and not to keep disturb them. it 
doesn't do much good to make accusations, especially if you can't make 
it clear what they're base on and their full significance, but it must 
be said that k. cause a great deal of harm to his own case by his 
behaviour towards the office director, he was a very influential man but 
now he might as well be struck off the list of those who might do 
anything for k. if the trial is mentioned, even just in passing, it's 
quite obvious that he's ignore it. these official are in many ways 
just like children. often, something quite harmless - although k.'s 
behaviour could unfortunately not be call harmless - will leave them 
feeling so offend that they will even stop talk with good friends 
of theirs, they turn away when they see them and do everything they can 
to oppose them. but then, with no particular reason, surprising 
enough, some little joke that was only ever attempt because everything 
seem so hopeless will make them laugh and they'll be reconciled. 
it's both difficult and hard at the same time to deal with them, and 
there's hardly any reason for it. it's sometimes quite astonish that 
a single, average life is enough to encompass so much that it's at all 
possible ever to have any success in one's work here. on the other 
hand, there are also dark moments, such as everyone has, when you think 
you've achieve nothing at all, when it seem that the only trial to 
come to a good end are those that were determined to have a good end 
from the start and would do so without any help, while all the other 
are lost despite all the running to and fro, all the effort, all the 
little, apparent success that gave such joy. then you no long feel 
very sure of anything and, if ask about a trial that was do well by 
its own nature but which was turn for the worse because you assist 
in it, would not even dare deny that. and even that is a kind of self- 
confidence, but then it's the only one that's left. lawyer are 
especially vulnerable to fit of depression of that sort - and they are 
no more than fit of depression of course - when a case is sudden 
taken out of their hand after they've been conduct it satisfactory 
for some time. that's probably the worst that can happen to a lawyer. 
it's not that the accused take the case away from him, that hardly ever 
happens, once a defendant has taken on a certain lawyer he has to stay 
with him whatever happens. how could he ever carry on by himself after 
he's taken on help from a lawyer? no, that just doesn't happen, but 
what do sometimes happen is that the trial take on a course where the 
lawyer may not go along with it. client and trial are both simply taken 
away from the lawyer; and then even contact with the court official 
won't help, however good they are, as they don't know anything 
themselves. the trial will have enter a stage where no more help can 
be given, where it's being process in court to which no-one has any 
access, where the defendant cannot even be contact by his lawyer. you 
come home one day and find all the document you've submitted, which 
you've work hard to create and which you had the best hope for, lie 
on the desk, they've been sent back as they can't be carry through to 
the next stage in the trial, they're just worthless scraps of paper. it 
doesn't mean that the case has been lost, not at all, or at least there 
is no decisive reason for supposing so, it's just that you don't know 
anything more about the case and won't be told anything of what's 
happening. well, case like that are the exceptions, i'm glad to say, 
and even if k.'s trial is one of them, it's still, for the time being, a 
long way off. but there was still plenty of opportunity for lawyer to 
get to work, and k. could be sure they would be made use of. as he had 
said, the time for submit document was still in the future and 
there was no rush to prepare them, it was much more important to start 
the initial discussion with the appropriate officials, and they had 
already taken place. with vary degree of success, it must be said. 
it was much better not to give away any detail before their time, as in 
that way k. could only be influence unfavourable and his hope might be 
raise or he might be made too anxious, better just to say that some 
individual have spoken very favourable and shown themselves very 
willing to help, although other have spoken less favourably, but even 
they have not in any way refuse to help. so all in all, the result 
are very encouraging, only you should certainly not draw any particular 
conclusion as all preliminary proceedings begin in the same way and it 
was only the way they develop further that would show what the value 
of these preliminary proceedings has been. anyway, nothing has been 
lost yet, and if we can succeed in get the office director, despite 
everything, on our side - and several action have been undertaken to 
this end - then everything is a clean wound, as a surgeon would say, and 
we can wait for the result with some comfort. 

when he start talk on in this way the lawyer was quite 
tireless. he went through it all again every time k. went to see him. 
there was always some progress, but he could never be told what sort of 
progress it was. the first set of document to be submit were being 
work on but still not ready, which usually turn out to be a great 
advantage the next time k. went to see him as the early occasion would 
have been a very bad time to put them in, which they could not then have 
known. if k., stupefy from all this talking, ever pointed out that 
even considering all these difficulty progress was very slow, the 
lawyer would object that progress was not slow at all, but that they 
might have progress far further if k. had come to him at the right 
time. but he had come to him late and that late would bring still 
further difficulties, and not only where time was concerned. the only 
welcome interrupt during these visit was always when leni contrived 
to bring the lawyer his tea while k. was there. then she would stand 
behind k. - pretend to watch the lawyer as he bent greedy over his 
cup, pour the tea in and drank - and secret let k. hold her hand. 
there was always complete silence. the lawyer drank. k. squeeze 
leni's hand and leni would sometimes dare to gently stroke k.'s hair. 
"still here, are you?" the lawyer would ask when he was ready. "i 
want to take the dishes away," said leni, they would give each other's 
hand a final squeeze, the lawyer would wipe his mouth and then start 
talk at k. again with renew energy. 

was the lawyer try to comfort k. or to confuse him? k. could 
not tell, but it seem clear to him that his defence was not in good 
hands. maybe everything the lawyer said was quite right, even though he 
obviously want to make himself as conspicuous as possible and probably 
had never even taken on a case as important as he said k.'s was. but it 
was still suspicious how he continual mention his personal contact 
with the civil servants. were they to be exploit solely for k.'s 
benefit? the lawyer never forgot to mention that they were dealing only 
with junior officials, which meant official who were dependent on 
others, and the direction taken in each trial could be important for 
their own furtherment. could it be that they were making use of the 
lawyer to turn trial in a certain direction, which would, of course, 
always be at the cost of the defendant? it certainly did not mean that 
they would do that in every trial, that was not likely at all, and there 
were probably also trial where they gave the lawyer advantage and all 
the room he need to turn it in the direction he wanted, as it would 
also be to their advantage to keep his reputation intact. if that 
really was their relationship, how would they direct k.'s trial which, 
as the lawyer had explained, was especially difficult and therefore 
important enough to attract great attention from the very first time it 
came to court? there could not be much doubt about what they would do. 
the first sign of it could already be seen in the fact that the first 
document still had not been submit even though the trial had already 
last several months, and that, according to the lawyer, everything was 
still in its initial stages, which was very effective, of course, in 
making the defendant passive and keeping him helpless. then he could be 
sudden surprise with the verdict, or at least with a notification 
that the hearing had not decided in his favour and the matter would be 
pass on to a higher office. 

it was essential that k. take a hand in it himself. on winter's 
mornings such as this, when he was very tired and everything drag 
itself lethargy through his head, this belief of his seem 
irrefutable. he no long felt the contempt for the trial that he had 
had earlier. if he had been alone in the world it would have been easy 
for him to ignore it, although it was also certain that, in that case, 
the trial would never have arisen in the first place. but now, his 
uncle had already drag him to see the lawyer, he had to take account 
of his family; his job was no long total separate from the progress 
of the trial, he himself had careless - with a certain, inexplicable 
complacency - mention it to acquaintance and other had learned about 
it in ways he did not know, his relationship with miss bürstner seem 
to be in trouble because of it. in short, he no long had any choice 
whether he would accept the trial or turn it down, he was in the middle 
of it and had to defend himself. if he was tired, then that was bad. 

but there was no reason to worry too much before he need to. he 
had been capable of working himself up to his high position in the bank 
in a relatively short time and to retain it with respect from everyone, 
now he simply had to apply some of the talent that had made that 
possible for him to the trial, and there was no doubt that it had to 
turn out well. the most important thing, if something was to be 
achieved, was to reject in advance any idea that he might be in any way 
guilty. there was no guilt. the trial was nothing but a big piece of 
business, just like he had already conclude to the benefit of the bank 
many times, a piece of business that conceal many lurk danger 
wait in ambush for him, as they usually did, and these danger would 
need to be defend against. if that was to be achieve then he must 
not entertain any idea of guilt, whatever he did, he would need to look 
after his own interests as close as he could. seen in this way, there 
was no choice but to take his representation away from the lawyer very 
soon, at best that very evening. the lawyer had told him, as he talk 
to him, that that was something unheard of and would probably do him a 
great deal of harm, but k. could not tolerate any impediment to his 
effort where his trial was concerned, and these impediment were 
probably cause by the lawyer himself. but once he had shaken off the 
lawyer the document would need to be submit straight away and, if 
possible, he would need to see to it that they were being dealt with 
every day. it would of course not be enough, if that was to be done, 
for k. to sit in the corridor with his hat under the bench like the 
others. day after day, he himself, or one of the women or somebody else 
on his behalf, would have to run after the official and force them to 
sit at their desk and study k.'s document instead of look out on 
the corridor through the grating. there could be no let-up in these 
efforts, everything would need to be organised and supervised, it was 
about time that the court came up against a defendant who knew how to 
defend and make use of his rights. 

but when k. had the confidence to try and do all this the 
difficulty of compose the document was too much for him. earlier, 
just a week or so before, he could only have felt shame at the thought 
of being made to write out such document himself; it had never enter 
his head that the task could also be difficult. he remember one 
morning when, already pile up with work, he sudden shove everything 
to one side and took a pad of paper on which he sketch out some of his 
thought on how document of this sort should proceed. perhaps he would 
offer them to that slow-witted lawyer, but just then the door of the 
manager's office open and the deputy-director enter the room with a 
loud laugh. k. was very embarrassed, although the deputy-director, of 
course, was not laugh at k.'s documents, which he knew nothing about, 
but at a joke he had just heard about the stock-exchange, a joke which 
need an illustration if it was to be understood, and now the deputy- 
director leant over k.'s desk, took his pencil from his hand, and drew 
the illustration on the writing pad that k. had intended for his idea 
about his case. 

k. now had no more thought of shame, the document had to be 
prepared and submitted. if, as was very likely, he could find no time 
to do it in the office he would have to do it at home at night. if the 
nights weren't enough he would have to take a holiday. above all, he could 
not stop half way, that was nonsense not only in business but always and 
everywhere. needless to say, the document would mean an almost endless 
amount of work. it was easy to come to the belief, not only for those 
of an anxious disposition, that it was impossible ever to finish it. 
this was not because of lazy or deceit, which were the only things 
that might have hinder the lawyer in prepare it, but because he did 
not know what the charge was or even what consequence it might bring, 
so that he had to remember every tiny action and event from the whole of 
his life, look at them from all side and check and reconsider 
them. it was also a very dishearten job. it would have been more 
suitable as a way of passing the long days after he had retired and 
become senile. but now, just when k. need to apply all his thought 
to his work, when he was still rising and already pose a threat to the 
deputy-director, when every hour pass so quick and he want to 
enjoy the brief evenings and nights as a young man, this was the time he 
had to start working out these documents. once more, he began to feel

he was a short, jolly man, a manufacturer who k. knew well. he

at last, k. was by himself. it did not enter his head to show

ledge beside it, held firm on to the handle and look down onto the 
square outside. the snow was still falling, the weather still had not 
brighten up at all. 

he remain a long time sitting in this way, not knowing what it 
actually was that made him so anxious, only occasional did he glance,

and how was he to succeed in conduct all this at the bank? it 
involved much more than just submit some document that he could 
probably prepare in a few days' leave, although it would have been great 
temerity to ask for time off from the bank just at that time, it was a 
whole trial and there was no way of seeing how long it might last. this 
was an enormous difficulty that had sudden been thrown into k.'s life! 

and was he supposed to be do the bank's work at a time like 
this? he look down at his desk. was he supposed to let people in to

consideration of this sort weaken his power of resistance, but it was 
still necessary not to deceive oneself and to see everything as clearly

the deputy director seem very good at appropriate everything

he went straight to the painter, who live in an outlying part of

didn't get this studio for nothing i'd have move out a long time ago." 
just then, a little voice, tender and anxious, call out from under the 
door, "titorelli, can we come in now?" "no," answer the painter. 
"not even just me, by myself?" the voice ask again. "not even just 
you," said the painter, as he went to the door and lock it. 

meanwhile, k. had been look round the room, if it had not been

many different colours. in the middle of the room a picture stood on an

behind k. was the window through which the fog made it impossible to see 
further than the snow cover roof of the neighbouring building. 

the turning of the key in the lock remind k. that he had not

"that's what the judge wanted," said the painter, "it's meant to be for 
a woman." the sight of the picture seem to make him feel like 
working, he roll up his shirtsleeves, picked up a few of the crayons,

that he had only gone there to investigate the painter. 

one of the girl behind the door start up again, and asked, 
"titorelli, is he go to go soon?" "quiet!" shout the painter at 
the door, "can't you see i'm talk with the gentleman?" but this was

"i'm sorry about that," said the painter as he return to k. k.

before me. it's a position that's always inherited. they can't use new 
people for it, the rule governing how the various grade of official 
are paint are so many and varied, and, above all, so secret that no-

trial now and then." "and how do you do that?" ask k., as if the

the painter had pull his seat close to the bed and continue in 
a subdued voice: "i forgot to ask you; what sort of acquittal is it you 
want? there are three possibilities; absolute acquittal, apparent 
acquittal and deferment. absolute acquittal is the best, of course, 
only there's nothing i could do to get that sort of outcome. i don't 
think there's anyone at all who could do anything to get an absolute 
acquittal. probably the only thing that could do that is if the accused 
is innocent. as you are innocent it could actually be possible and you 
could depend on your innocence alone. in that case you won't need me or 
any other kind of help." 

at first, k. was astonish at this orderly explanation, but then, 
just as quiet as the painter, he said, "i think you're contradict 
yourself." "how's that?" ask the painter patiently, leaning back with 
a smile. this smile made k. feel as if he were examine not the words 
of the painter but seek out inconsistent in the procedure of the

remark early that the court cannot be approach with reasoned

contradictions," said the painter. "we're talk about two different 
things here, there's what it say in the law and there's what i know 
from my own experience, you shouldn't get the two confused. i've never 
seen it in writing, but the law does, of course, say on the one hand 
that the innocent will be set free, but on the other hand it doesn't say 
that the judge can be influenced. but in my experience it's the other 
way round. i don't know of any absolute acquittal but i do know of 
many times when a judge has been influenced. it's possible, of course, 
that there was no innocence in any of the case i know about. but is 
that likely? not a single innocent defendant in so many cases? when i

court case at home, and the judge that came to his studio talk about 
the court, in our circle nobody talks about anything else; i hardly 
ever got the chance to go to court myself but always made use of it when 
i could, i've listen to countless trial at important stage in their 
development, i've follow them close as far as they could be 
followed, and i have to say that i've never seen a single acquittal." 
"so. not a single acquittal," said k., as if talk to himself and his 
hopes. "that confirm the impression i already have of the court. so 
there's no point in it from this side either. they could replace the 
whole court with a single hangman." "you shouldn't generalise," said 
the painter, dissatisfied, "i've only been talk about my own 
experience." "well that's enough," said k., "or have you heard of any 
acquittal that happen earlier?" "they say there have been some 
acquittal earlier," the painter answered, "but it's very hard to be 
sure about it. the court don't make their final conclusion public,

about these early case are just legends. but most of them did 
involve absolute acquittals, you can believe that, but they can't be 
proved. on the other hand, you shouldn't forget all about them either, 
i'm sure there is some truth to them, and they are very beautiful, i've 
paint a few pictures myself depict these legends." "my assessment 
will not be alter by mere legends," said k. "i don't suppose it's 
possible to cite these legend in court, is it?" the painter laughed. 
"no, you can't cite them in court," he said. "then there's no point in 
talk about them," said k., he wanted, for the time being, to accept 
anything the painter told him, even if he thought it unlikely or

disprove it, he would have achieve as much as he could if the painter 
would help him in any way even if his help would not be decisive. as a 
result, he said, "so let's pay no more attention to absolute acquittal, 
but you mention two other possibilities." "apparent acquittal and 
deferment. they're the only possibilities," said the painter. "but 
before we talk about them, would you not like to take your coat off? 
you must be hot." "yes," said k., who until then had paid attention to 
nothing but the painter's explanations, but now that he had had the heat 
pointed out to him his brow began to sweat heavily. "it's almost 
unbearable." the painter nod as if he understood k.'s discomfort 
very well. "could we not open the window?" ask k. "no," said the 
painter. "it's only a fixed pane of glass, it can't be opened." k. now

do want to i can open one of my doors, or even both of them." k. was 
slightly console by this explanation and look around to see where the

i had to hide it behind the bed." only then was k. able to see the 
little door in the wall. "it's really much too small for a studio

would make. "i had to arrange things as well as i could. that's 
obviously a very bad place for the bed, in front of the door. for

through the door by the bed, and i've even given him a key to this door 
so that he can wait for me here in the studio when i'm not home. 
although nowadays he usually come early in the morning when i'm still 
asleep. and of course, it always wake me up when i hear the door 
open beside the bed, however fast asleep i am. if you could hear the 
way i curse him as he climb over my bed in the morning you'd lose all 
respect for judges. i suppose i could take the key away from him but 
that'd only make things worse. it only take a tiny effort to break any 
of the door here off their hinges." all the time the painter was 
speaking, k. was considering whether he should take off his coat, but he 
finally realise that, if he didn't do so, he would be quite unable to 
stay here any longer, so he took off his frock coat and lay it on his 
knee so that he could put it back on again as soon as the conversation 
was over. he had hardly done this when one of the girl call out, 
"now he's taken his coat off!" and they could all be heard pressing 
around the gap in the plank to see the spectacle for themselves. "the 
girl think i'm go to paint your portrait," said the painter, "and 
that's why you're taking your coat off." "i see," said k., only 
slightly amuse by this, as he felt little better than he had before 
even though he now sat in his shirtsleeves. with some irritation he 
asked, "what did you say the two other possibility were?" he had 
already forgotten the terms used. "apparent acquittal and deferment," 
said the painter. "it's up to you which one you choose. you can get 
either of them if i help you, but it'll take some effort of course, the 
difference between them is that apparent acquittal needs concentrated 
effort for a while and that defer take much less effort but it has 
to be sustained. now then, apparent acquittal. if that's what you want 
i'll write down an assertion of your innocence on a piece of paper. the 
text for an assertion of this sort was pass down to me from my father 
and it's quite unassailable. i take this assertion round to the judge 
i know. so i'll start off with the one i'm current painting, and put 
the assertion to him when he come for his sitting this evening. i'll 
lay the assertion in front of him, explain that you're innocent and give

guarantee, it's a real one and it's binding." the painter's eyes seem 
to show some reproach of k. for wanting to impose that sort of 
responsibility on him. "that would be very kind of you", said k. "and 
would the judge then believe you and nonetheless not pass an absolute 
acquittal?" "it's like i just said," answer the painter. "and 
anyway, it's not entire sure that all the judge would believe me, 
many of them, for instance, might want me to bring you to see them 
personally. so then you'd have to come along too. but at least then, 
if that happens, the matter is half way won, especially as i'd teach you 
in advance exactly how you'd need to act with the judge concerned, of 
course. what also happens, though, is that there are some judge who'll 
turn me down in advance, and that's worse. i'll certainly make several 
attempts, but still, we'll have to forget about them, but at least we 
can afford to do that as no one judge can pass the decisive verdict. 
then when i've got enough judges' signature on this document i take it 
to the judge who's concerned with your case. i might even have his 
signature already, in which case things develop a bit quick than they 
would do otherwise. but there aren't usually many hold ups from then 
on, and that's the time that the defendant can feel most confident. 
it's odd, but true, that people feel more confidence in this time than 
they do after they've been acquitted. there's no particular exertion 
need now. when he has the document assert the defendant's 
innocence, guarantee by a number of other judges, the judge can acquit 
you without any worries, and although there are still several 
formality to be gone through there's no doubt that that's what he'll 
do as a favour to me and several other acquaintances. you, however, 
walk out the court and you're free." "so, then i'll be free," said k., 
hesitantly. "that's right," said the painter, "but only apparently free 
or, to put it a better way, temporary free, as the most junior judges, 
the one i know, they don't have the right to give the final acquittal. 
only the highest judge can do that, in the court that's quite out of reach 
for you, for me and for all of us. we don't know how things look there 
and, incidentally, we don't want to know. the right to acquit people 
is a major privilege and our judge don't have it, but they do have the 
right to free people from the indictment. that's to say, if they're 
free in this way then for the time being the charge is withdrawn but 
it's still hanging over their head and it only take an order from 
higher up to bring it back into force. and as i'm in such good contact 
with the court i can also tell you how the difference between absolute 
and apparent acquittal is described, just in a superficial way, in the 
directive to the court offices. if there's an absolute acquittal all 
proceedings should stop, everything disappear from the process, not 
just the indict but the trial and even the acquittal disappears, 
everything just disappears. with an apparent acquittal it's different. 
when that happens, nothing has change except that the case for your 
innocence, for your acquittal and the grounds for the acquittal have 
been made stronger. apart from that, proceedings go on as before, the 
court offices continue their business and the case get pass to higher 
courts, get pass back down to the lower court and so on, backwards 
and forwards, sometimes faster, sometimes slower, to and fro. it's 
impossible to know exactly what's happening while this is go on. 
seen from outside it can sometimes seem that everything has been long 
since forgotten, the document have been lost and the acquittal is 
complete. no-one familiar with the court would believe it. no 
document ever get lost, the court forget nothing. one day - no-one 
expect it - some judge or other pick up the document and looks more 
close at them, he notice that this particular case is still active, 
and orders the defendant's immediate arrest. i've been talk here as 
if there's a long delay between apparent acquittal and re-arrest, that 
is quite possible and i do know of case like that, but it's just as 
likely that the defendant go home after he's been acquit and find 
somebody there wait to re-arrest him. then, of course, his life as a 
free man is at an end." "and do the trial start over again?" ask 
k., finding it hard to believe. "the trial will always start over 
again," said the painter, "but there is, once again as before, the

has to muster all his strength and mustn't give up." the painter said

shoulder had drop somewhat, gave on him. "but to get a second

the painter, "is that not hard to get than the first time?" "as far

for certain. you mean, do you, that the second arrest would have an 
adverse influence on the judge and the verdict he pass on the 
defendant? that's not how it happens. when the acquittal is pass the 
judge are already aware that re-arrest is likely. so when it happen 
it has hardly any effect. but there are countless other reason why the 
judges' mood and their legal acumen in the case can be altered, and 
effort to obtain the second acquittal must therefore be suit to the 
new conditions, and generally just as vigorous as the first." "but this 
second acquittal will once again not be final," said k., shake his 
head. "of course not," said the painter, "the second acquittal is 
follow by the third arrest, the third acquittal by the fourth arrest 
and so on. that's what is meant by the term apparent acquittal." k. 
was silent. "you clearly don't think an apparent acquittal offer much 
advantage," said the painter, "perhaps defer would suit you better. 
would you like me to explain what defer is about?" k. nodded. the 
painter had leant back and spread himself out in his chair, his 
nightshirt was wide open, he had pushed his hand inside and was stroke 
his breast and his sides. "deferment," said the painter, look 
vague in front of himself for a while as if try to find a perfectly 
appropriate explanation, "deferment consist of keeping proceedings 
permanent in their earliest stages. to do that, the accused and those 
helping him need to keep in continuous personal contact with the court, 
especially those helping him. i repeat, this doesn't require so much 
effort as get an apparent acquittal, but it probably require a lot 
more attention. you must never let the trial out of your sight, you 
have to go and see the appropriate judge at regular interval as well as 
when something in particular come up and, whatever you do, you have to 
try and remain friendly with him; if you don't know the judge personally 
you have to influence him through the judge you do know, and you have 
to do it without give up on the direct discussions. as long as you 
don't fail to do any of these things you can be reasonably sure the 
trial won't get past its first stages. the trial doesn't stop, but the 
defendant is almost as certain of avoid conviction as if he'd been 
acquitted. compare with an apparent acquittal, defer has the 
advantage that the defendant's future is less uncertain, he's safe from 
the shock of being sudden re-arrested and doesn't need to fear the 
exertion and stress involved in get an apparent acquittal just when 
everything else in his life would make it most difficult. defer 
do have certain disadvantage of its own though, too, and they 
shouldn't be under-estimated. i don't mean by this that the defendant 
is never free, he's never free in the proper sense of the word with an 
apparent acquittal either. there's another disadvantage. proceedings 
can't be prevent from moving forward unless there are some at least 
ostensible reason given. so something needs to seem to be happening 
when look at from the outside. this means that from time to time 
various injunction have to be obeyed, the accused has to be questioned, 
investigate have to take place and so on. the trial's been 
artificial constrained inside a tiny circle, and it has to be 
continuous spun round within it. and that, of course, bring with it 
certain unpleasantnesse for the accused, although you shouldn't imagine 
they're all that bad. all of this is just for show, the interrogations, 
for instance, they're only very short, if you ever don't have the time 
or don't feel like go to them you can offer an excuse, with some 
judge you can even arrange the injunction together a long time in 
advance, in essence all it means is that, as the accused, you have to 
report to the judge from time to time." even while the painter was 
speak those last words k. had laid his coat over his arm and had 
stood up. immediately, from outside the door, there was a cry of 'he's 
standing up now!'. "are you leave already?" ask the painter, who 
had also stood up. "it must be the air that's driving you out. i'm 
very sorry about that. there's still a lot i need to tell you. i had 
to put everything very brief but i hope at least it was all clear." 
"oh yes," said k., whose head was ache from the effort of listening. 
despite this affirm the painter sum it all up once more, as if 
he want to give k. something to console him on his way home. "both 
have in common that they prevent the defendant being convicted," he 
said. "but they also prevent his being properly acquitted," said k. 
quietly, as if ashamed to acknowledge it. "you've got it, in essence," 
said the painter quickly. k. placed his hand on his winter overcoat but

girl could induce him to put his coat on, even though they were already

advantage and disadvantages. everything has to be careful weigh

"i'll come back here again soon," said k., who had sudden decided to

"in fact he became quite cross when i start to talk about block to

start at a different point from where i do. a difference of opinion,

chapter nine 
in the cathedral 

a very important italy business contact of the bank had come to 
visit the city for the first time and k. was given the task of showing 
him some of its cultural sights. at any other time he would have seen 
this job as an honour but now, when he was finding it hard even to 
maintain his current position in the bank, he accept it only with 
reluctance. every hour that he could not be in the office was a cause 
of concern for him, he was no long able to make use of his time in the 
office anything like as well as he had previously, he spent many hour 
merely pretend to do important work, but that only increase his 
anxiety about not being in the office. then he sometimes thought he saw 
the deputy director, who was always watching, come into k.'s office, sit 
at his desk, look through his papers, receive client who had almost 
become old friends of k., and lure them away from him, perhaps he even 
discover mistakes, mistake that seem to threaten k. from a thousand 
directions when he was at work now, and which he could no long avoid. 
so now, if he was ever ask to leave the office on business or even 
need to make a short business trip, however much an honour it seem - 
and task of this sort happen to have increase substantially recently 
- there was always the suspicion that they want to get him out of his 
office for a while and check his work, or at least the idea that they 
thought he was dispensable. it would not have been difficult for him to 
turn down most of these jobs, but he did not dare to do so because, if 
his fear had the slightest foundation, turning the job down would have 
been an acknowledgement of them. for this reason, he never demur 
from accept them, and even when he was ask to go on a tire 
business trip lasting two days he said nothing about have to go out in 
the rainy autumn weather when he had a severe chill, just in order to 
avoid the risk of not being ask to go. when, with a rage headache, 
he arrive back from this trip he learned that he had been chosen to 
accompany the italy business contact the following day. the 
temptation for once to turn the job down was very great, especially as 
it had no direct connection with business, but there was no deny that 
social obligation towards this business contact were in themselves 
important enough, only not for k., who knew quite well that he need 
some success at work if he was to maintain his position there and 
that, if he fail in that, it would not help him even if this italy 
somehow found him quite charming; he did not want to be remove from his 
workplace for even one day, as the fear of not being allow back in was 
too great, he knew full well that the fear was exaggerate but it still 
made him anxious. however, in this case it was almost impossible to 
think of an acceptable excuse, his knowledge of italy was not great 
but still good enough; the decide factor was that k. had early known 
a little about art history and this had become widely known around the 
bank in extremely exaggerate form, and that k. had been a member of the 
society for the preservation of city monuments, albeit only for business 
reasons. it was said that this italy was an art lover, so the choice 
of k. to accompany him was a matter of course. 

it was a very rainy and stormy morning when k., in a foul temper

already there. "i'll come straight away," said k. he put a small 
dictionary in his pocket, took a guide to the city's tourist site under

into the office so early and was able to be of service immediately, 
nobody could seriously have expect that of him. the deputy director's

as k. enter the reception room two men stood up from the deep 
armchair where they had been sitting. the director gave him a friendly

at exactly half past nine, just when he was about to leave, there

when he enter the central nave to go back to where he had left
