and k. would certainly not have notice this little pulpit if 
there had not been a lamp fasten above it, which usually meant there 
was a sermon about to be given. so was a sermon to be given now? in 
this empty church? k. look down at the steps which, pressed close 
against the column, led up to the pulpit. they were so narrow they 
seem to be there as decoration on the column rather than for anyone to 
use. but under the pulpit - k. grin in astonishment - there really 
was a priest standing with his hand on the handrail ready to climb the 
steps and look at k. then he nod very slightly, so that k. 
cross himself and genuflect as he should have done earlier. with a 
little swing, the priest went up into the pulpit with short fast steps. 
was there really a sermon about to begin? maybe the man in the cassock 
had not been really so demented, and had meant to lead k.'s way to the 
preacher, which in this empty church would have been very necessary. 
and there was also, somewhere in front of a picture of the virgin mary, 
an old woman who should have come to hear the sermon. and if there was 
to be a sermon why had it not been introduce on the organ? but the 
organ remain quiet and merely look out weak from the dark of 
its great height. 

k. now considered whether he should leave as quick as possible, 
if he did not do it now there would be no chance of do so during the 
sermon and he would have to stay there for as long as it lasted, he had 
lost so much time when he should have been in his office, there had long 
been no need for him to wait for the italy any longer, he look at 
his watch, it was eleven. but could there really be a sermon given? 
could k. constitute the entire congregation? how could he when he was 
just a stranger who want to look at the church? that, basically, was 
all he was. the idea of a sermon, now, at eleven o'clock, on a workday, 
in hideous weather, was nonsense. the priest - there was no doubt that 
he was a priest, a young man with a smooth, dark face - was clearly 
go up there just to put the lamp out after somebody had lit it by 
mistake. 

but there had been no mistake, the priest seem rather to check 
that the lamp was lit and turn it a little higher, then he slow 
turn to face the front and leant down on the balustrade gripping its 
angular rail with both hands. he stood there like that for a while and, 
without turning his head, look around. k. had move back a long way 
and leant his elbow on the front pew. somewhere in the church - he 
could not have said exactly where - he could make out the man in the 
cassock hunch under his bent back and at peace, as if his work were 
completed. in the cathedral it was now very quiet! but k. would have 
to disturb that silence, he had no intention of stay there; if it was 
the priest's duty to preach at a certain time regardless of the 
circumstances then he could, and he could do it without k.'s taking 
part, and k.'s presence would do nothing to augment the effect of it. 
so k. began slow to move, felt his way on tiptoe along the pew, 
arrive at the broad aisle and went along it without being disturbed, 
except for the sound of his steps, however light, which rang out on the 
stone floor and resound from the vaulting, quiet but continuous at a 
repeating, regular pace. k. felt slightly abandoned as, probably 
observe by the priest, he walk by himself between the empty pews, and 
the size of the cathedral seem to be just at the limit of what a man 
could bear. when he arrive back at where he had been sitting he did 
not hesitate but simply reach out for the album he had left there and 
took it with him. he had nearly left the area cover by pew and was 
close to the empty space between himself and the exit when, for the 
first time, he heard the voice of the priest. a powerful and 
experienced voice. it pierce through the reach of the cathedral 
ready wait for it! but the priest was not calling out to the 
congregation, his cry was quite unambiguous and there was no escape from 
it, he call "josef k.!" 

k. stood still and look down at the floor. in theory he was 
still free, he could have carry on walking, through one of three dark 
little wooden door not far in front of him and away from there. it 
would simply mean he had not understood, or that he had understood but 
chose not to pay attention to it. but if he once turn round he would 
be trapped, then he would have acknowledge that he had understood 
perfectly well, that he really was the josef k. the priest had call to 
and that he was willing to follow. if the priest had call out again 
k. would certainly have carry on out the door, but everything was 
silent as k. also waited, he turn his head slightly as he want to 
see what the priest was do now. he was merely standing in the pulpit 
as before, but it was obvious that he had seen k. turn his head. if k. 
did not now turn round completely it would have been like a child 
play hide and seek. he did so, and the priest beckon him with his 
finger. as everything could now be done openly he ran - because of 
curiosity and the wish to get it over with - with long flying leap 
towards the pulpit. at the front pew he stopped, but to the priest he 
still seem too far away, he reach out his hand and pointed sharp 
down with his finger to a place immediately in front of the pulpit. and 
k. did as he was told, standing in that place he had to bend his head a 
long way back just to see the priest. "you are josef k.," said the 
priest, and raise his hand from the balustrade to make a gesture whose 
meaning was unclear. "yes," said k., he considered how freely he had 
always given his name in the past, for some time now it had been a 
burden to him, now there were people who knew his name whom he had never 
seen before, it had been so nice first to introduce yourself and only 
then for people to know who you were. "you have been accused," said the 
priest, especially gently. "yes," said k., "so i have been informed." 
"then you are the one i am look for," said the priest. "i am the 
prison chaplain." "i see," said k. "i had you summon here," said the 
priest, "because i want to speak to you." "i knew nothing of that," 
said k. "i came here to show the cathedral to a gentleman from italy." 
"that is beside the point," said the priest. "what are you holding in 
your hand? is it a prayer book?" "no," answer k., "it's an album of 
the city's tourist sights." "put it down," said the priest. k. threw 
it away with such force that it flap open and roll across the 
floor, tear its pages. "do you know your case is go badly?" ask 
the priest. "that's how it seem to me too," said k. "i've expend a 
lot of effort on it, but so far with no result. although i do still 
have some document to submit." "how do you imagine it will end?" ask 
the priest. "at first i thought it was bound to end well," said k., 
"but now i have my doubt about it. i don't know how it will end. do 
you know?" "i don't," said the priest, "but i fear it will end badly. 
you are considered guilty. your case will probably not even go beyond a 
minor court. provisional at least, your guilt is seen as proven." 
"but i'm not guilty," said k., "there's been a mistake. how is it even 
possible for someone to be guilty. we're all human being here, one 
like the other." "that is true," said the priest, "but that is how the 
guilty speak." "do you presume i'm guilty too?" ask k. "i make no 
presumption about you," said the priest. "i thank you for that," said 
k. "but everyone else involved in these proceedings has something 
against me and presume i'm guilty. they even influence those who 
aren't involved. my position get hard all the time." "you don't 
understand the facts," said the priest, "the verdict do not come 
suddenly, proceedings continue until a verdict is reach gradually." 
"i see," said k., lower his head. "what do you intend to do about 
your case next?" ask the priest. "i still need to find help," said 
k., raise his head to see what the priest thought of this. "there are 
still certain possibility i haven't yet made use of." "you look for 
too much help from people you don't know," said the priest 
disapprovingly, "and especially from women. can you really not see 
that's not the help you need?" "sometimes, in fact quite often, i could 
believe you're right," said k., "but not always. women have a lot of 
power. if i could persuade some of the women i know to work together 
with me then i would be certain to succeed. especially in a court like 
this that seem to consist of nothing but woman-chasers. show the 
examine judge a woman in the distance and he'll run right over the 
desk, and the accused, just to get to her as soon as he can." the 
priest lower his head down to the balustrade, only now did the roof 
over the pulpit seem to press him down. what sort of dreadful weather 
could it be outside? it was no long just a dull day, it was deepest 
night. none of the stain glass in the main window shed even a flicker 
of light on the dark of the walls. and this was the moment when the 
man in the cassock chose to put out the candle on the main altar, one 
by one. "are you cross with me?" ask k. "maybe you don't know what 
sort of court it is you serve." he received no answer. "well, it's 
just my own experience," said k. above him there was still silence. "i 
didn't mean to insult you," said k. at that, the priest scream down 
at k.: "can you not see two steps in front of you?" he shout in 
anger, but it was also the scream of one who see another fall and, 
shock and without thinking, scream against his own will. 

the two men, then, remain silent for a long time. in the 
dark beneath him, the priest could not possibly have seen k.

little lamp. why did the priest not come down? he had not given a 
sermon, he had only told k. a few things which, if he follow them

obtain some acceptable piece of advice that could make all the

influence the proceedings but how to break free of them, how to evade

knew of such a possibility he might, if k. ask him, let him know about 
it, even though he was part of the court himself and even though, when 
k. had criticise the court, he had held down his gentle nature and 
actually shout at k. 

"would you not like to come down here?" ask k. "if you're not 
go to give a sermon come down here with me." "now i can come down,"

k. wait for him at the foot of the steps. while he was still on

beside each other in the dark of one of the side naves. "that make 
you an exception among all those who belong to the court. i can trust 
you more than any of the other i've seen. i can speak openly with 
you." "don't fool yourself," said the priest. "how would i be fool 
myself?" ask k. "you fool yourself in the court," said the priest, 
"it talks about this self-deceit in the opening paragraph to the law. 
in front of the law there is a doorkeeper. a man from the countryside 
come up to the door and ask for entry. but the doorkeeper say he 
can't let him in to the law right now. the man think about this, and 
then he ask if he'll be able to go in later on. 'that's possible,' 
say the doorkeeper, 'but not now'. the gateway to the law is open as 
it always is, and the doorkeeper has step to one side, so the man 
bends over to try and see in. when the doorkeeper notice this he 
laugh and says, 'if you're tempt give it a try, try and go in even 
though i say you can't. careful though: i'm powerful. and i'm only the 
lowliest of all the doormen. but there's a doorkeeper for each of the 
rooms and each of them is more powerful than the last. it's more than i 
can stand just to look at the third one.' the man from the country had 
not expect difficulty like this, the law was supposed to be 
accessible for anyone at any time, he thinks, but now he looks more

long thin tartar-beard, and he decide it's better to wait until he has 
permission to enter. the doorkeeper give him a stool and let him sit

requests. the doorkeeper often question him, ask about where he's 
from and many other things, but these are disinterested question such 
as great men ask, and he always end up by telling him he still can't 
let him in. the man had come well equip for his journey, and use 
everything, however valuable, to bribe the doorkeeper. he accept 
everything, but as he do so he says, 'i'll only accept this so that 
you don't think there's anything you've fail to do'. over many years, 
the man watches the doorkeeper almost without a break. he forget about 
the other doormen, and begin to think this one is the only thing 
stopping him from gain access to the law. over the first few years he 
curse his unhappy condition out loud, but later, as he become old, he 
just grumble to himself. he become senile, and as he has come to know 
even the flea in the doorkeeper's fur collar over the years that he has 
been study him he even ask them to help him and change the 
doorkeeper's mind. finally his eyes grow dim, and he no long know 
whether it's really get dark or just his eyes that are deceive 
him. but he seem now to see an inextinguishable light begin to shine 
from the dark behind the door. he doesn't have long to live now. 
just before he dies, he bring together all his experience from all this 
time into one question which he has still never put to the doorkeeper. 
he beckon to him, as he's no long able to raise his stiff body. the 
doorkeeper has to bend over deep as the difference in their size has 
change very much to the disadvantage of the man. 'what is it you want 
to know now?' ask the doorkeeper, 'you're insatiable.' 'everyone wants 
access to the law,' say the man, 'how come, over all these years, no- 
one but me has ask to be let in?' the doorkeeper can see the man's 
come to his end, his hearing has faded, and so, so that he can be heard, 
he shout to him: 'nobody else could have got in this way, as this 
entrance was meant only for you. now i'll go and close it'." 

"so the doorkeeper cheat the man," said k. immediately, who had 
been captivate by the story. "don't be too quick," said the priest, 
"don't take somebody else's opinion without check it. i told you the 
story exactly as it was written. there's nothing in there about 
cheating." "but it's quite clear," said k., "and your first 
interpretation of it was quite correct. the doorkeeper gave him the 
information that would release him only when it could be of no more 
use." "he didn't ask him before that," said the priest, "and don't 
forget he was only a doorkeeper, and as doorkeeper he did his duty." 
"what make you think he did his duty?" ask k., "he didn't. it might 
have been his duty to keep everyone else away, but this man is who the 
door was intended for and he ought to have let him in." "you're not 
pay enough attention to what was written and you're change the 
story," said the priest. "according to the story, there are two 
important things that the doorkeeper explain about access to the law, 
one at the beginning, one at the end. at one place he say he can't 
allow him in now, and at the other he say this entrance was intended 
for him alone. if one of the statement contradict the other you 
would be right and the doorkeeper would have cheat the man from the 
country. but there is no contradiction. on the contrary, the first 
statement even hint at the second. you could almost say the doorkeeper 
went beyond his duty in that he offer the man some prospect of being 
admitted in the future. throughout the story, his duty seem to have 
been merely to turn the man away, and there are many commentator who 
are surprise that the doorkeeper offer this hint at all, as he seem 
to love exactitude and keeps strict guard over his position. he stays 
at his post for many years and doesn't close the gate until the very 
end, he's very conscious of the importance of his service, as he says, 
'i'm powerful,' he has respect for his superiors, as he says, 'i'm only 
the lowliest of the doormen', he's not talkative, as through all these 
years the only question he ask are 'disinterested', he's not 
corruptible, as when he's offer a gift he says, 'i'll only accept this 
so that you don't think there's anything you've fail to do,' as far as 
fulfill his duty go he can be neither ruffle nor begged, as it 
say about the man that, 'he tire the doorkeeper with his requests', 
even his external appearance suggest a pedantic character, the big 
hooked nose and the long, thin, black tartar-beard. how could any 
doorkeeper be more faithful to his duty? but in the doorkeeper's 
character there are also other features which might be very useful for 
those who seek entry to the law, and when he hint at some possibility 
in the future it always seem to make it clear that he might even go 
beyond his duty. there's no deny he's a little simple minded, and 
that make him a little conceited. even if all he said about his power 
and the power of the other doorkeeper and how not even he could bear 
the sight of them - i say even if all these assertion are right, the 
way he make them show that he's too simple and arrogant to understand 
properly. the commentator say about this that, 'correct understanding 
of a matter and a misunderstanding of the same matter are not mutual 
exclusive'. whether they're right or not, you have to concede that his 
simplicity and arrogance, however little they show, do weaken his 
function of guard the entrance, they are defect in the doorkeeper's 
character. you also have to consider that the doorkeeper seem to be 
friendly by nature, he isn't always just an official. he make a joke 
right at the beginning, in that he invite the man to enter at the same 
time as maintain the ban on his entering, and then he doesn't send 
him away but give him, as it say in the text, a stool to sit on and 
let him stay by the side of the door. the patience with which he put 
up with the man's request through all these years, the little 
questioning sessions, accept the gifts, his polite when he put 
up with the man curse his fate even though it was the doorkeeper who 
cause that fate - all these things seem to want to arouse our sympathy. 
not every doorkeeper would have behave in the same way. and finally, 
he let the man beckon him and he bends deep down to him so that he can 
put his last question. there's no more than some slight impatience - 
the doorkeeper know everything's come to its end - shown in the words, 
'you're insatiable'. there are many commentator who go even further in 
explain it in this way and think the words, 'you're insatiable' are 
an expression of friendly admiration, albeit with some condescension. 
however you look at it the figure of the doorkeeper come out 
different from how you might think." "you know the story better than 
i do and you've known it for longer," said k. they were silent for a 
while. and then k. said, "so you think the man was not cheated, do 
you?" "don't get me wrong," said the priest, "i'm just point out the 
different opinion about it. you shouldn't pay too much attention to 
people's opinions. the text cannot be altered, and the various opinion 
are often no more than an expression of despair over it. there's even 
one opinion which say it's the doorkeeper who's been cheated." "that 
do seem to take things too far," said k. "how can they argue the 
doorkeeper has been cheated?" "their argument," answer the priest, 
"is base on the simplicity of the doorkeeper. they say the doorkeeper 
doesn't know the inside of the law, only the way into it where he just 
walk up and down. they see his idea of what's inside the law as 
rather childish, and suppose he's afraid himself of what he wants to 
make the man frightened of. yes, he's more afraid of it than the man, 
as the man wants nothing but to go inside the law, even after he's heard 
about the terrible doormen there, in contrast to the doorkeeper who 
doesn't want to go in, or at least we don't hear anything about it. on 
the other hand, there are those who say he must have already been inside 
the law as he has been taken on into its service and that could only 
have been done inside. that can be counter by supposing he could have 
been given the job of doorkeeper by somebody calling out from inside, 
and that he can't have gone very far inside as he couldn't bear the 
sight of the third doorkeeper. nor, through all those years, do the

forbidden to do so, but he hasn't said anything about that either. all 
this seem to show he doesn't know anything about what the inside looks 
like or what it means, and that that's why he's being deceived. but 
he's also being deceive by the man from the country as he's this man's 
subordinate and doesn't know it. there's a lot to indicate that he 
treat the man as his subordinate, i expect you remember, but those who 
hold this view would say it's very clear that he really is his 
subordinate. above all, the free man is superior to the man who has to 
serve another. now, the man really is free, he can go wherever he 
wants, the only thing forbidden to him is entry into the law and, what's 
more, there's only one man forbidding him to do so - the doorkeeper. if

who the door's intended for. this is another way in which he's his

say nothing at all to show there's been any change." "that is well 
substantiated," said k., who had been repeating some parts of the

deceived. although that do not mean i've abandoned what i thought 
early as the two version are, to some extent, not incompatible. it's

the man had been cheated. if the doorkeeper understand clearly, then 
there could be some doubt about it, but if the doorkeeper has been 
deceive then the man is bound to believe the same thing. that would 
mean the doorkeeper is not a cheat but so simple-minded that he ought to 
be dismiss from his job immediately; if the doorkeeper is mistaken it 
will do him no harm but the man will be harm immensely." "there 
you've found another opinion," said the priest, "as there are many who 
say the story doesn't give anyone the right to judge the doorkeeper. 
however he might seem to us he is still in the service of the law, so he 
belong to the law, so he's beyond what man has a right to judge. in 
this case we can't believe the doorkeeper is the man's subordinate. 
even if he has to stay at the entrance into the law his service make 
him incomparable more than if he live freely in the world. the man has 
come to the law for the first time and the doorkeeper is already there. 
he's been given his position by the law, to doubt his worth would be to 
doubt the law." "i can't say i'm in complete agreement with this view," 
said k. shake his head, "as if you accept it you'll have to accept 
that everything said by the doorkeeper is true. but you've already 
explain very fully that that's not possible." "no," said the priest, 
"you don't need to accept everything as true, you only have to accept it 
as necessary." "depressing view," said k. "the lie made into the rule 
of the world." 

k. said that as if it were his final word but it was not his 
conclusion. he was too tired to think about all the ramification of 
the story, and the sort of thought they led him into were not familiar 
to him, unrealistic things, things better suit for official of the 
court to discuss than for him. the simple story had lost its shape, he 
want to shake it off, and the priest who now felt quite compassionate 
allow this and accept k.'s remark without comment, even though his 
view was certainly very different from k.'s. 

in silence, they carry on walking for some time, k. stay close 
beside the priest without knowing where he was. the lamp in his hand 
had long since gone out. once, just in front of him, he thought he 
could see the statue of a saint by the glitter of the silver on it,

go until then, but he immediately said, 
"yes, certainly, i have to go. i'm the chief clerk in a bank and there

the priest. "you were so friendly to me early on," said k., "and you

"well, yes," said k., "you need to understand that." "first, you need

said k., and went close to the priest, it was not so important for him 
to go straight back to the bank as he had made out, he could very well 
stay where he was. "so that means i belong to the court," said the 
priest. "so why would i want anything from you? the court doesn't want 
anything from you. it accept you when you come and it let you go when 
you leave."

end 

the evening before k.'s thirty-first birthday - it was about nine 
o'clock in the evening, the time when the street were quiet - two men 
came to where he lived. in frock coats, pale and fat, wearing top hat 
that look like they could not be taken off their heads. after some 
brief formality at the door of the flat when they first arrived, the 
same formality were repeated at greater length at k.'s door. he had 
not been notify they would be coming, but k. sat in a chair near the 
door, dress in black as they were, and slow put on new glove which 
stretch tight over his finger and behave as if he were expect 
visitors. he immediately stood up and look at the gentlemen 
inquisitively. "you've come for me then, have you?" he asked. the 
gentlemen nodded, one of them indicate the other with the top hand now 
in his hand. k. told them he had been expect a different visitor. 
he went to the window and look once more down at the dark street. 
most of the window on the other side of the street were also dark 
already, many of them had the curtains closed. in one of the window on 
the same floor where there was a light on, two small children could be 
seen play with each other inside a playpen, unable to move from where 
they were, reach out for each other with their little hands. "some 
ancient, unimportant actor - that's what they've sent for me," said k. 
to himself, and look round once again to confirm this to himself. 
"they want to sort me out as cheap as they can." k. sudden turn 
round to face the two men and asked, "what theatre do you play in?" 
"theatre?" ask one of the gentlemen, turning to the other for 
assistance and pull in the corner of his mouth. the other made a 
gesture like someone who was dumb, as if he were struggle with some 
organism cause him trouble. "you're not properly prepared to answer 
questions," said k. and went to fetch his hat. 

as soon as they were on the stairs the gentlemen want to take 
k.'s arms, but k. said "wait till we're in the street, i'm not ill." 
but they wait only until the front door before they took his arms in a 
way that k. had never experienced before. they kept their shoulder 
close behind his, did not turn their arms in but twist them around the 
entire length of k.'s arms and took hold of his hand with a grasp that 
was formal, experienced and could not be resisted. k. was held stiff 
and upright between them, they form now a single unit so that if any 
one of them had been knock down all of them must have fallen. they 
form a unit of the sort that normally can be form only by matter 
that is lifeless. 

whenever they pass under a lamp k. try to see his companion 
more clearly, as far as was possible when they were pressed so close 
together, as in the dim light of his room this had been hardly possible. 
"maybe they're tenors," he thought as he saw their big double chins. 
the cleanliness of their face disgust him. he could see the hand 
that clean them, passing over the corner of their eyes, rubbing at 
their upper lips, scratch out the crease on those chins. 

when k. notice that, he stopped, which meant the other had to 
stop too; they were at the edge of an open square, devoid of people but 
decorate with flower beds. "why did they send you, of all people!" he 
cry out, more a shout than a question. the two gentleman clearly knew 
no answer to give, they waited, their free arms hanging down, like 
nurse when the patient needs to rest. "i will go no further," said k. 
as if to see what would happen. the gentlemen did not need to make any 
answer, it was enough that they did not loosen their grip on k. and 
try to move him on, but k. resist them. "i'll soon have no need of 
much strength, i'll use all of it now," he thought. he thought of the 
flies that tear their leg off struggle to get free of the flypaper. 
"these gentleman will have some hard work to do". 

just then, miss bürstner came up into the square in front of them 
from the steps leading from a small street at a lower level. it was not 
certain that it was her, although the similarity was, of course, great. 
but it did not matter to k. whether it was certainly her anyway, he just 
became sudden aware that there was no point in his resistance. there 
would be nothing heroic about it if he resisted, if he now cause 
trouble for these gentlemen, if in defend himself he sought to enjoy 
his last glimmer of life. he start walking, which pleased the 
gentlemen and some of their pleasure convey itself to him. now they 
permit him to decide which direction they took, and he decided to 
take the direction that follow the young woman in front of them, not 
so much because he want to catch up with her, nor even because he 
want to keep her in sight for as long as possible, but only so that he 
would not forget the reproach she represent for him. "the only thing 
i can do now," he said to himself, and his thought was confirmed by the 
equal length of his own steps with the steps of the two others, "the 
only thing i can do now is keep my common sense and do what's need 
right till the end. i always want to go at the world and try and do 
too much, and even to do it for something that was not too cheap. that 
was wrong of me. should i now show them i learned nothing from facing 
trial for a year? should i go out like someone stupid? should i let 
anyone say, after i'm gone, that at the start of the proceedings i 
want to end them, and that now that they've end i want to start them 
again? i don't want anyone to say that. i'm grateful they sent these 
unspeaking, uncomprehend men to go with me on this journey, and that 
it's been left up to me to say what's necessary". 

meanwhile, the young woman had turn off into a side street, but 
k. could do without her now and let his companion lead him. all three 
of them now, in complete agreement, went over a bridge in the light of 
the moon, the two gentlemen were willing to yield to each little 
movement made by k. as he move slightly towards the edge and direct 
the group in that direction as a single unit. the moonlight glitter 
and quiver in the water, which divide itself around a small island 
cover in a densely-piled mass of foliage and tree and bushes. 
beneath them, now invisible, there were gravel path with comfortable 
bench where k. had stretch himself out on many summer's days. "i 
didn't actually want to stop here," he said to his companions, shame by 
their compliance with his wishes. behind k.'s back one of them seem 
to quiet criticise the other for the misunderstanding about stopping, 
and then they went on. they went on up through several street where 
policemen were walking or standing here and there; some in the distance 
and then some very close. one of them with a bushy moustache, his hand 
on the grip of his sword, seem to have some purpose in approach 
the group, which was hardly unsuspicious. the two gentlemen stopped, 
the policeman seem about to open his mouth, and then k. drove his 
group forceful forward. several times he look back cautious to 
see if the policeman was following; but when they had a corner between 
themselves and the policeman k. began to run, and the two gentlemen, 
despite being seriously short of breath, had to run with him. 

in this way they quick left the built up area and found 
themselves in the field which, in this part of town, began almost 
without any transition zone. there was a quarry, empty and abandoned, 
near a building which was still like those in the city. here the men 
stopped, perhaps because this had always been their destination or 
perhaps because they were too exhaust to run any further. here they 
release their hold on k., who just wait in silence, and took their 
top hat off while they look round the quarry and wipe the sweat off 
their brow with their handkerchiefs. the moonlight lay everywhere with 
the natural peace that is grant to no other light. 

after exchange a few courtesy about who was to carry out the 
next task - the gentlemen did not seem to have been allocate specific 
function - one of them went to k. and took his coat, his waistcoat, and 
finally his shirt off him. k. made an involuntary shiver, at which the 
gentleman gave him a gentle, reassure tap on the back. then he 
careful fold the things up as if they would still be needed, even if 
not in the near future. he did not want to expose k. to the chilly 
night air without moving though, so he took him under the arm and walk 
up and down with him a little way while the other gentleman look round 
the quarry for a suitable place. when he had found it he made a sign 
and the other gentleman escort him there. it was near the rockface, 
there was a stone lie there that had broken loose. the gentlemen sat 
k. down on the ground, leant him against the stone and settled his head 
down on the top of it. despite all the effort they went to, and despite 
all the co-operation shown by k., his demeanour seem very forced and 
hard to believe. so one of the gentlemen ask the other to grant him a 
short time while he put k. in position by himself, but even that did 
nothing to make it better. in the end they left k. in a position that 
was far from the best of the one they had try so far. then one of 
the gentlemen open his frock coat and from a sheath hanging on a belt 
stretch across his waistcoat he withdrew a long, thin, double-edged 
butcher's knife which he held up in the light to test its sharpness. 
the repulsive courtesy began once again, one of them pass the knife 
over k. to the other, who then pass it back over k. to the first. k. 
now knew it would be his duty to take the knife as it pass from hand 
to hand above him and thrust it into himself. but he did not do it, 
instead he twist his neck, which was still free, and look around. 
he was not able to show his full worth, was not able to take all the 
work from the official bodies, he lack the rest of the strength he 
need and this final shortcoming was the fault of whoever had deny it 
to him. as he look round, he saw the top floor of the building next 
to the quarry. he saw how a light flicker on and the two halves of a 
window open out, somebody, made weak and thin by the height and the 
distance, leant sudden far out from it and stretch his arms out even 
further. who was that? a friend? a good person? somebody who was 
taking part? somebody who want to help? was he alone? was it 
everyone? would anyone help? were there objection that had been 
forgotten? there must have been some. the logic cannot be refuted, but 
someone who wants to live will not resist it. where was the judge he'd 
never seen? where was the high court he had never reached? he raise 
both hand and spread out all his fingers. 

but the hand of one of the gentleman were laid on k.'s throat, 
while the other pushed the knife deep into his heart and twist it 
there, twice. as his eyesight failed, k. saw the two gentlemen cheek by 
cheek, close in front of his face, watch the result. "like a dog!" he 
said, it was as if the shame of it should outlive him.
